CHAPTER I .
TREATS OF THE PLACE WHERE OLIVER TWIST WAS BORN AND OF THE CIRCUMSTANCES ATTENDING HIS BIRTH Among other public building in a certain town , which for many reason it will be prudent to refrain from mentioning , and to which I will assign no fictitious name , there be one anciently common to most town , great or small : to wit , a workhouse ; and in this workhouse be bear ; on a day and date which I need not trouble myself to repeat , inasmuch a it can be of no possible consequence to the reader , in this stage of the business at all event ; the item of mortality whose name be prefix to the head of this chapter .
For a long time after it be usher into this world of sorrow and trouble , by the parish surgeon , it remain a matter of considerable doubt whether the child would survive to bear any name at all ; in which case it be somewhat more than probable that these memoir would never have appear ; or , if they have , that be comprise within a couple of page , they would have possess the inestimable merit of be the most concise and faithful specimen of biography , extant in the literature of any age or country .
Although I be not dispose to maintain that the be bear in a workhouse , be in itself the most fortunate and enviable circumstance that can possibly befall a human be , I do mean to say that in this particular instance , it be the best thing for Oliver Twist that could by possibility have occur .
The fact be , that there be considerable difficulty in induce Oliver to take upon himself the office of respiration , —a troublesome practice , but one which custom have render necessary to our easy existence ; and for some time he lay gasp on a little flock mattress , rather unequally poise between this world and the next : the balance be decidedly in favour of the latter .
Now , if , during this brief period , Oliver have be surround by careful grandmother , anxious aunt , experienced nurse , and doctor of profound wisdom , he would most inevitably and indubitably have be kill in no time .
There be nobody by , however , but a pauper old woman , who be render rather misty by an unwonted allowance of beer ; and a parish surgeon who do such matter by contract ; Oliver and Nature fight out the point between them .
The result be , that , after a few struggle , Oliver breathe , sneeze , and proceed to advertise to the inmate of the workhouse the fact of a new burden have be impose upon the parish , by set up a loud a cry a could reasonably have be expect from a male infant who have not be possess of that very useful appendage , a voice , for a much long space of time than three minute and a quarter .
As Oliver give this first proof of the free and proper action of his lung , the patchwork coverlet which be carelessly fling over the iron bedstead , rustle ; the pale face of a young woman be raise feebly from the pillow ; and a faint voice imperfectly articulate the word , “ Let me see the child , and die. ” The surgeon have be sit with his face turn towards the fire : give the palm of his hand a warm and a rub alternately .
As the young woman speak , he rise , and advance to the bed ’ s head , say , with more kindness than might have be expect of him : “ Oh , you must not talk about die yet. ” “ Lor bless her dear heart , no ! ” interpose the nurse , hastily deposit in her pocket a green glass bottle , the content of which she have be taste in a corner with evident satisfaction .
“ Lor bless her dear heart , when she have live as long a I have , sir , and have thirteen child of her own , and all on ’ em dead except two , and them in the wurkus with me , she ’ ll know good than to take on in that way , bless her dear heart !
Think what it be to be a mother , there ’ s a dear young lamb do. ” Apparently this consolatory perspective of a mother ’ s prospect fail in produce it due effect .
The patient shake her head , and stretch out her hand towards the child .
The surgeon deposit it in her arm .
She imprint her cold white lip passionately on it forehead ; pass her hand over her face ; gaze wildly round ; shudder ; fell back—and die .
They chafe her breast , hand , and temple ; but the blood have stop forever .
They talk of hope and comfort .
They have be stranger too long .
“ It ’ s all over , Mrs. Thingummy ! ” say the surgeon at last .
“ Ah , poor dear , so it be ! ” say the nurse , pick up the cork of the green bottle , which have fall out on the pillow , a she stoop to take up the child .
“ Poor dear ! ” “ You needn ’ t mind send up to me , if the child cry , nurse , ” say the surgeon , put on his glove with great deliberation .
“ It ’ s very likely it _will_ be troublesome .
Give it a little gruel if it is. ” He put on his hat , and , pause by the bed-side on his way to the door , add , “ She be a good-looking girl , too ; where do she come from ? ” “ She be bring here last night , ” reply the old woman , “ by the overseer ’ s order .
She be find lie in the street .
She have walk some distance , for her shoe be wear to piece ; but where she come from , or where she be go to , nobody knows. ” The surgeon lean over the body , and raise the left hand .
“ The old story , ” he say , shake his head : “ no wedding-ring , I see .
Ah !
Good-night ! ” The medical gentleman walk away to dinner ; and the nurse , have once more applied herself to the green bottle , sit down on a low chair before the fire , and proceed to dress the infant .
What an excellent example of the power of dress , young Oliver Twist be !
Wrapped in the blanket which have hitherto form his only covering , he might have be the child of a nobleman or a beggar ; it would have be hard for the haughty stranger to have assign him his proper station in society .
But now that he be envelop in the old calico robe which have grow yellow in the same service , he be badge and ticket , and fell into his place at once—a parish child—the orphan of a workhouse—the humble , half-starved drudge—to be cuff and buffet through the world—despised by all , and pity by none .
Oliver cry lustily .
If he could have know that he be an orphan , leave to the tender mercy of church-wardens and overseer , perhaps he would have cry the louder .
CHAPTER II .
TREATS OF OLIVER TWIST ’ S GROWTH , EDUCATION , AND BOARD For the next eight or ten month , Oliver be the victim of a systematic course of treachery and deception .
He be bring up by hand .
The hungry and destitute situation of the infant orphan be duly report by the workhouse authority to the parish authority .
The parish authority inquire with dignity of the workhouse authority , whether there be no female then domicile in “ the house ” who be in a situation to impart to Oliver Twist , the consolation and nourishment of which he stand in need .
The workhouse authority reply with humility , that there be not .
Upon this , the parish authority magnanimously and humanely resolve , that Oliver should be “ farm , ” or , in other word , that he should be dispatch to a branch-workhouse some three mile off , where twenty or thirty other juvenile offender against the poor-laws , roll about the floor all day , without the inconvenience of too much food or too much clothing , under the parental superintendence of an elderly female , who receive the culprit at and for the consideration of sevenpence-halfpenny per small head per week .
Sevenpence-halfpenny ’ s worth per week be a good round diet for a child ; a great deal may be get for sevenpence-halfpenny , quite enough to overload it stomach , and make it uncomfortable .
The elderly female be a woman of wisdom and experience ; she know what be good for child ; and she have a very accurate perception of what be good for herself .
So , she appropriate the great part of the weekly stipend to her own use , and consign the rise parochial generation to even a short allowance than be originally provide for them .
Thereby find in the low depth a deep still ; and prove herself a very great experimental philosopher .
Everybody know the story of another experimental philosopher who have a great theory about a horse be able to live without eating , and who demonstrate it so well , that he have get his own horse down to a straw a day , and would unquestionably have render him a very spirited and rampacious animal on nothing at all , if he have not die , four-and-twenty hour before he be to have have his first comfortable bait of air .
Unfortunately for the experimental philosophy of the female to whose protect care Oliver Twist be deliver over , a similar result usually attend the operation of _her_ system ; for at the very moment when the child have contrive to exist upon the small possible portion of the weak possible food , it do perversely happen in eight and a half case out of ten , either that it sicken from want and cold , or fell into the fire from neglect , or get half-smothered by accident ; in any one of which case , the miserable little be be usually summon into another world , and there gather to the father it have never know in this .
Occasionally , when there be some more than usually interesting inquest upon a parish child who have be overlook in turn up a bedstead , or inadvertently scald to death when there happen to be a washing—though the latter accident be very scarce , anything approach to a wash be of rare occurrence in the farm—the jury would take it into their head to ask troublesome question , or the parishioner would rebelliously affix their signature to a remonstrance .
But these impertinence be speedily check by the evidence of the surgeon , and the testimony of the beadle ; the former of whom have always open the body and find nothing inside ( which be very probable indeed ) , and the latter of whom invariably swear whatever the parish want ; which be very self-devotional .
Besides , the board make periodical pilgrimage to the farm , and always send the beadle the day before , to say they be go .
The child be neat and clean to behold , when _they_ go ; and what more would the people have !
It can not be expect that this system of farm would produce any very extraordinary or luxuriant crop .
Oliver Twist ’ s ninth birthday find him a pale thin child , somewhat diminutive in stature , and decidedly small in circumference .
But nature or inheritance have implant a good sturdy spirit in Oliver ’ s breast .
It have have plenty of room to expand , thanks to the spare diet of the establishment ; and perhaps to this circumstance may be attribute his have any ninth birth-day at all .
Be this a it may , however , it be his ninth birthday ; and he be keep it in the coal-cellar with a select party of two other young gentleman , who , after participate with him in a sound thrashing , have be lock up for atrociously presume to be hungry , when Mrs. Mann , the good lady of the house , be unexpectedly startle by the apparition of Mr. Bumble , the beadle , strive to undo the wicket of the garden-gate .
“ Goodness gracious !
Is that you , Mr. Bumble , sir ? ” say Mrs. Mann , thrust her head out of the window in well-affected ecstasy of joy .
“ ( Susan , take Oliver and them two brat upstairs , and wash ’ em directly .
) —My heart alive !
Mr. Bumble , how glad I be to see you , sure-ly ! ” Now , Mr. Bumble be a fat man , and a choleric ; so , instead of respond to this open-hearted salutation in a kindred spirit , he give the little wicket a tremendous shake , and then bestow upon it a kick which could have emanate from no leg but a beadle ’ s .
“ Lor , only think , ” say Mrs. Mann , run out , —for the three boy have be remove by this time , — “ only think of that !
That I should have forget that the gate be bolt on the inside , on account of them dear child !
Walk in sir ; walk in , pray , Mr. Bumble , do , sir. ” Although this invitation be accompany with a curtsey that might have soften the heart of a church-warden , it by no mean mollify the beadle .
“ Do you think this respectful or proper conduct , Mrs. Mann , ” inquire Mr. Bumble , grasp his cane , “ to keep the parish officer a wait at your garden-gate , when they come here upon porochial business with the porochial orphan ?
Are you aweer , Mrs. Mann , that you be , a I may say , a porochial delegate , and a stipendiary ? ” “ I ’ m sure Mr. Bumble , that I be only a tell one or two of the dear child a be so fond of you , that it be you a come , ” reply Mrs. Mann with great humility .
Mr. Bumble have a great idea of his oratorical power and his importance .
He have display the one , and vindicate the other .
He relax .
“ Well , well , Mrs. Mann , ” he reply in a calmer tone ; “ it may be a you say ; it may be .
Lead the way in , Mrs. Mann , for I come on business , and have something to say. ” Mrs. Mann usher the beadle into a small parlour with a brick floor ; place a seat for him ; and officiously deposit his cocked hat and cane on the table before him .
Mr. Bumble wipe from his forehead the perspiration which his walk have engender , glance complacently at the cocked hat , and smile .
Yes , he smile .
Beadles be but men : and Mr. Bumble smile .
“ Now don ’ t you be offend at what I ’ m a go to say , ” observe Mrs. Mann , with captivate sweetness .
“ You ’ ve have a long walk , you know , or I wouldn ’ t mention it .
Now , will you take a little drop of somethink , Mr. Bumble ? ” “ Not a drop .
Nor a drop , ” say Mr. Bumble , wave his right hand in a dignified , but placid manner .
“ I think you will , ” say Mrs. Mann , who have notice the tone of the refusal , and the gesture that have accompany it .
“ Just a leetle drop , with a little cold water , and a lump of sugar. ” Mr. Bumble cough .
“ Now , just a leetle drop , ” say Mrs. Mann persuasively .
“ What be it ? ” inquire the beadle .
“ Why , it ’ s what I ’ m oblige to keep a little of in the house , to put into the blessed infant ’ Daffy , when they ain ’ t well , Mr. Bumble , ” reply Mrs. Mann a she open a corner cupboard , and take down a bottle and glass .
“ It ’ s gin .
I ’ ll not deceive you , Mr. B. It ’ s gin. ” “ Do you give the child Daffy , Mrs. Mann ? ” inquire Bumble , follow with his eye the interesting process of mixing .
“ Ah , bless ’ em , that I do , dear a it be , ” reply the nurse .
“ I couldn ’ t see ’ em suffer before my very eyes , you know sir. ” “ No ” ; say Mr. Bumble approvingly ; “ no , you could not .
You be a humane woman , Mrs. Mann. ” ( Here she set down the glass . )
“ I shall take a early opportunity of mention it to the board , Mrs. Mann. ” ( He draw it towards him . )
“ You feel a a mother , Mrs. Mann. ” ( He stir the gin-and-water . )
“ I—I drink your health with cheerfulness , Mrs. Mann ” ; and he swallow half of it .
“ And now about business , ” say the beadle , take out a leathern pocket-book .
“ The child that be half-baptized Oliver Twist , be nine year old to-day. ” “ Bless him ! ” interpose Mrs. Mann , inflame her left eye with the corner of her apron .
“ And notwithstanding a offer reward of ten pound , which be afterwards increase to twenty pound .
Notwithstanding the most superlative , and , I may say , supernat ’ ral exertion on the part of this parish , ” say Bumble , “ we have never be able to discover who be his father , or what be his mother ’ s settlement , name , or condition. ” Mrs. Mann raise her hand in astonishment ; but add , after a moment ’ s reflection , “ How come he to have any name at all , then ? ” The beadle draw himself up with great pride , and say , “ I inwented it. ” “ You , Mr. Bumble ! ” “ I , Mrs. Mann .
We name our fondling in alphabetical order .
The last be a S , —Swubble , I name him .
This be a T , —Twist , I name _him_ .
The next one come will be Unwin , and the next Vilkins .
I have get name ready make to the end of the alphabet , and all the way through it again , when we come to Z. ” “ Why , you ’ re quite a literary character , sir ! ” say Mrs. Mann .
“ Well , well , ” say the beadle , evidently gratify with the compliment ; “ perhaps I may be .
Perhaps I may be , Mrs. Mann. ” He finish the gin-and-water , and add , “ Oliver be now too old to remain here , the board have determine to have him back into the house .
I have come out myself to take him there .
So let me see him at once. ” “ I ’ ll fetch him directly , ” say Mrs. Mann , leave the room for that purpose .
Oliver , have have by this time a much of the outer coat of dirt which encrust his face and hand , remove , a could be scrub off in one washing , be lead into the room by his benevolent protectress .
“ Make a bow to the gentleman , Oliver , ” say Mrs. Mann .
Oliver make a bow , which be divide between the beadle on the chair , and the cocked hat on the table .
“ Will you go along with me , Oliver ? ” say Mr. Bumble , in a majestic voice .
Oliver be about to say that he would go along with anybody with great readiness , when , glance upward , he catch sight of Mrs. Mann , who have get behind the beadle ’ s chair , and be shake her fist at him with a furious countenance .
He take the hint at once , for the fist have be too often impressed upon his body not to be deeply impressed upon his recollection .
“ Will she go with me ? ” inquired poor Oliver .
“ No , she can ’ t , ” reply Mr. Bumble .
“ But she ’ ll come and see you sometimes. ” This be no very great consolation to the child .
Young a he be , however , he have sense enough to make a feint of feel great regret at go away .
It be no very difficult matter for the boy to call tear into his eye .
Hunger and recent ill-usage be great assistant if you want to cry ; and Oliver cry very naturally indeed .
Mrs. Mann give him a thousand embrace , and what Oliver want a great deal more , a piece of bread and butter , le he should seem too hungry when he get to the workhouse .
With the slice of bread in his hand , and the little brown-cloth parish cap on his head , Oliver be then lead away by Mr. Bumble from the wretched home where one kind word or look have never light the gloom of his infant year .
And yet he burst into an agony of childish grief , a the cottage-gate close after him .
Wretched a be the little companion in misery he be leave behind , they be the only friends he have ever know ; and a sense of his loneliness in the great wide world , sink into the child ’ s heart for the first time .
Mr. Bumble walk on with long stride ; little Oliver , firmly grasp his gold-laced cuff , trot beside him , inquire at the end of every quarter of a mile whether they be “ nearly there. ” To these interrogation Mr. Bumble return very brief and snappish reply ; for the temporary blandness which gin-and-water awakens in some bosom have by this time evaporate ; and he be once again a beadle .
Oliver have not be within the wall of the workhouse a quarter of an hour , and have scarcely complete the demolition of a second slice of bread , when Mr. Bumble , who have hand him over to the care of an old woman , return ; and , tell him it be a board night , inform him that the board have say he be to appear before it forthwith .
Not have a very clearly defined notion of what a live board be , Oliver be rather astound by this intelligence , and be not quite certain whether he ought to laugh or cry .
He have no time to think about the matter , however ; for Mr. Bumble give him a tap on the head , with his cane , to wake him up : and another on the back to make him lively : and bid him to follow , conduct him into a large white-washed room , where eight or ten fat gentleman be sit round a table .
At the top of the table , seat in an arm-chair rather high than the rest , be a particularly fat gentleman with a very round , red face .
“ Bow to the board , ” say Bumble .
Oliver brush away two or three tear that be linger in his eye ; and see no board but the table , fortunately bow to that .
“ What ’ s your name , boy ? ” say the gentleman in the high chair .
Oliver be frighten at the sight of so many gentleman , which make him tremble : and the beadle give him another tap behind , which make him cry .
These two cause make him answer in a very low and hesitating voice ; whereupon a gentleman in a white waistcoat say he be a fool .
Which be a capital way of raise his spirit , and put him quite at his ease .
“ Boy , ” say the gentleman in the high chair , “ listen to me .
You know you ’ re an orphan , I suppose ? ” “ What ’ s that , sir ? ” inquire poor Oliver .
“ The boy _is_ a fool—I thought he be , ” say the gentleman in the white waistcoat .
“ Hush ! ” say the gentleman who have speak first .
“ You know you ’ ve get no father or mother , and that you be bring up by the parish , don ’ t you ? ” “ Yes , sir , ” reply Oliver , weep bitterly .
“ What be you cry for ? ” inquire the gentleman in the white waistcoat .
And to be sure it be very extraordinary .
What _could_ the boy be cry for ?
“ I hope you say your prayer every night , ” say another gentleman in a gruff voice ; “ and pray for the people who fee you , and take care of you—like a Christian. ” “ Yes , sir , ” stammer the boy .
The gentleman who speak last be unconsciously right .
It would have be very like a Christian , and a marvellously good Christian too , if Oliver have pray for the people who feed and take care of _him_ .
But he hadn ’ t , because nobody have teach him .
“ Well !
You have come here to be educate , and teach a useful trade , ” say the red-faced gentleman in the high chair .
“ So you ’ ll begin to pick oakum to-morrow morning at six o ’ clock , ” add the surly one in the white waistcoat .
For the combination of both these blessing in the one simple process of pick oakum , Oliver bow low by the direction of the beadle , and be then hurry away to a large ward ; where , on a rough , hard bed , he sob himself to sleep .
What a novel illustration of the tender law of England !
They let the pauper go to sleep !
Poor Oliver !
He little think , a he lay sleep in happy unconsciousness of all around him , that the board have that very day arrive at a decision which would exercise the most material influence over all his future fortune .
But they have .
And this be it : The member of this board be very sage , deep , philosophical men ; and when they come to turn their attention to the workhouse , they find out at once , what ordinary folk would never have discovered—the poor people like it !
It be a regular place of public entertainment for the poorer class ; a tavern where there be nothing to pay ; a public breakfast , dinner , tea , and supper all the year round ; a brick and mortar elysium , where it be all play and no work .
“ Oho ! ” say the board , look very know ; “ we be the fellow to set this to right ; we ’ ll stop it all , in no time. ” So , they establish the rule , that all poor people should have the alternative ( for they would compel nobody , not they ) , of be starve by a gradual process in the house , or by a quick one out of it .
With this view , they contract with the water-works to lay on an unlimited supply of water ; and with a corn-factor to supply periodically small quantity of oatmeal ; and issue three meal of thin gruel a day , with an onion twice a week , and half a roll of Sundays .
They make a great many other wise and humane regulation , have reference to the lady , which it be not necessary to repeat ; kindly undertake to divorce poor married people , in consequence of the great expense of a suit in Doctors ’ Commons ; and , instead of compel a man to support his family , a they have theretofore do , take his family away from him , and make him a bachelor !
There be no say how many applicant for relief , under these last two head , might have start up in all class of society , if it have not be couple with the workhouse ; but the board be long-headed men , and have provide for this difficulty .
The relief be inseparable from the workhouse and the gruel ; and that frightened people .
For the first six month after Oliver Twist be remove , the system be in full operation .
It be rather expensive at first , in consequence of the increase in the undertaker ’ s bill , and the necessity of take in the clothes of all the pauper , which flutter loosely on their waste , shrunken form , after a week or two ’ s gruel .
But the number of workhouse inmate get thin as well a the pauper ; and the board be in ecstasy .
The room in which the boy be feed , be a large stone hall , with a copper at one end : out of which the master , dress in an apron for the purpose , and assist by one or two woman , ladle the gruel at mealtime .
Of this festive composition each boy have one porringer , and no more—except on occasion of great public rejoicing , when he have two ounce and a quarter of bread besides .
The bowl never want washing .
The boy polish them with their spoon till they shine again ; and when they have perform this operation ( which never take very long , the spoon be nearly as large a the bowl ) , they would sit star at the copper , with such eager eye , a if they could have devour the very brick of which it be compose ; employ themselves , meanwhile , in suck their finger most assiduously , with the view of catch up any stray splash of gruel that might have be cast thereon .
Boys have generally excellent appetite .
Oliver Twist and his companion suffer the torture of slow starvation for three month : at last they get so voracious and wild with hunger , that one boy , who be tall for his age , and hadn ’ t be use to that sort of thing ( for his father have keep a small cook-shop ) , hint darkly to his companion , that unless he have another basin of gruel per diem , he be afraid he might some night happen to eat the boy who sleep next him , who happen to be a weakly youth of tender age .
He have a wild , hungry eye ; and they implicitly believe him .
A council be hold ; lot be cast who should walk up to the master after supper that evening , and ask for more ; and it fell to Oliver Twist .
The evening arrive ; the boy take their place .
The master , in his cook ’ s uniform , station himself at the copper ; his pauper assistant range themselves behind him ; the gruel be serve out ; and a long grace be say over the short common .
The gruel disappear ; the boy whisper each other , and wink at Oliver ; while his next neighbor nudge him .
Child a he be , he be desperate with hunger , and reckless with misery .
He rise from the table ; and advance to the master , basin and spoon in hand , say : somewhat alarm at his own temerity : “ Please , sir , I want some more. ” The master be a fat , healthy man ; but he turn very pale .
He gaze in stupefied astonishment on the small rebel for some second , and then clung for support to the copper .
The assistant be paralyse with wonder ; the boy with fear .
“ What ! ” say the master at length , in a faint voice .
“ Please , sir , ” reply Oliver , “ I want some more. ” The master aim a blow at Oliver ’ s head with the ladle ; pinion him in his arm ; and shriek aloud for the beadle .
The board be sit in solemn conclave , when Mr. Bumble rush into the room in great excitement , and address the gentleman in the high chair , say , “ Mr .
Limbkins , I beg your pardon , sir !
Oliver Twist have ask for more ! ” There be a general start .
Horror be depict on every countenance .
“ For _more_ ! ” say Mr. Limbkins .
“ Compose yourself , Bumble , and answer me distinctly .
Do I understand that he ask for more , after he have eat the supper allot by the dietary ? ” “ He do , sir , ” reply Bumble .
“ That boy will be hang , ” say the gentleman in the white waistcoat .
“ I know that boy will be hung. ” Nobody controvert the prophetic gentleman ’ s opinion .
An animated discussion take place .
Oliver be order into instant confinement ; and a bill be next morning paste on the outside of the gate , offer a reward of five pound to anybody who would take Oliver Twist off the hand of the parish .
In other word , five pound and Oliver Twist be offer to any man or woman who want an apprentice to any trade , business , or call .
“ I never be more convinced of anything in my life , ” say the gentleman in the white waistcoat , a he knock at the gate and read the bill next morning : “ I never be more convinced of anything in my life , than I be that that boy will come to be hung. ” As I purpose to show in the sequel whether the white waistcoated gentleman be right or not , I should perhaps mar the interest of this narrative ( suppose it to possess any at all ) , if I venture to hint just yet , whether the life of Oliver Twist have this violent termination or no .
CHAPTER III .
RELATES HOW OLIVER TWIST WAS VERY NEAR GETTING A PLACE WHICH WOULD NOT HAVE BEEN A SINECURE For a week after the commission of the impious and profane offence of ask for more , Oliver remain a close prisoner in the dark and solitary room to which he have be consign by the wisdom and mercy of the board .
It appear , at first sight not unreasonable to suppose , that , if he have entertain a become feeling of respect for the prediction of the gentleman in the white waistcoat , he would have establish that sage individual ’ s prophetic character , once and for ever , by tie one end of his pocket-handkerchief to a hook in the wall , and attach himself to the other .
To the performance of this feat , however , there be one obstacle : namely , that pocket-handkerchiefs be decided article of luxury , have be , for all future time and age , remove from the nose of pauper by the express order of the board , in council assemble : solemnly give and pronounce under their hand and seal .
There be a still great obstacle in Oliver ’ s youth and childishness .
He only cry bitterly all day ; and , when the long , dismal night come on , spread his little hand before his eye to shut out the darkness , and crouch in the corner , try to sleep : ever and anon wake with a start and tremble , and draw himself close and closer to the wall , a if to feel even it cold hard surface be a protection in the gloom and loneliness which surround him .
Let it not be suppose by the enemy of “ the system , ” that , during the period of his solitary incarceration , Oliver be deny the benefit of exercise , the pleasure of society , or the advantage of religious consolation .
As for exercise , it be nice cold weather , and he be allow to perform his ablution every morning under the pump , in a stone yard , in the presence of Mr. Bumble , who prevent his catch cold , and cause a tingling sensation to pervade his frame , by repeat application of the cane .
As for society , he be carry every other day into the hall where the boy din , and there sociably flog a a public warning and example .
And so far from be deny the advantage of religious consolation , he be kick into the same apartment every evening at prayer-time , and there permit to listen to , and console his mind with , a general supplication of the boy , contain a special clause , therein insert by authority of the board , in which they entreat to be make good , virtuous , content , and obedient , and to be guard from the sin and vice of Oliver Twist : whom the supplication distinctly set forth to be under the exclusive patronage and protection of the power of wickedness , and an article direct from the manufactory of the very Devil himself .
It chance one morning , while Oliver ’ s affair be in this auspicious and comfortable state , that Mr. Gamfield , chimney-sweep , go his way down the High Street , deeply cogitate in his mind his way and mean of pay certain arrears of rent , for which his landlord have become rather press .
Mr. Gamfield ’ s most sanguine estimate of his finance could not raise them within full five pound of the desired amount ; and , in a specie of arithmetical desperation , he be alternately cudgel his brain and his donkey , when pass the workhouse , his eye encounter the bill on the gate .
“ Wo—o ! ” say Mr. Gamfield to the donkey .
The donkey be in a state of profound abstraction : wondering , probably , whether he be destine to be regale with a cabbage-stalk or two when he have dispose of the two sack of soot with which the little cart be laden ; so , without notice the word of command , he jog onward .
Mr. Gamfield growl a fierce imprecation on the donkey generally , but more particularly on his eye ; and , run after him , bestow a blow on his head , which would inevitably have beat in any skull but a donkey ’ s .
Then , catch hold of the bridle , he give his jaw a sharp wrench , by way of gentle reminder that he be not his own master ; and by these mean turn him round .
He then give him another blow on the head , just to stun him till he come back again .
Having complete these arrangement , he walk up to the gate , to read the bill .
The gentleman with the white waistcoat be stand at the gate with his hand behind him , after have deliver himself of some profound sentiment in the board-room .

CHAPTER IV .
OLIVER , BEING OFFERED ANOTHER PLACE , MAKES HIS FIRST ENTRY INTO PUBLIC LIFE In great family , when an advantageous place can not be obtain , either in possession , reversion , remainder , or expectancy , for the young man who be grow up , it be a very general custom to send him to sea .
The board , in imitation of so wise and salutary an example , take counsel together on the expediency of ship off Oliver Twist , in some small trading vessel bind to a good unhealthy port .
This suggest itself a the very best thing that could possibly be do with him : the probability be , that the skipper would flog him to death , in a playful mood , some day after dinner , or would knock his brain out with an iron bar ; both pastime be , a be pretty generally know , very favourite and common recreation among gentleman of that class .
The more the case present itself to the board , in this point of view , the more manifold the advantage of the step appear ; so , they come to the conclusion that the only way of provide for Oliver effectually , be to send him to sea without delay .

CHAPTER V. OLIVER MINGLES WITH NEW ASSOCIATES .
GOING TO A FUNERAL FOR THE FIRST TIME , HE FORMS AN UNFAVOURABLE NOTION OF HIS MASTER ’ S BUSINESS Oliver , be leave to himself in the undertaker ’ s shop , set the lamp down on a workman ’ s bench , and gaze timidly about him with a feeling of awe and dread , which many people a good deal old than he will be at no loss to understand .
An unfinished coffin on black tressels , which stand in the middle of the shop , look so gloomy and death-like that a cold tremble come over him , every time his eye wander in the direction of the dismal object : from which he almost expect to see some frightful form slowly rear it head , to drive him mad with terror .
Against the wall be range , in regular array , a long row of elm board cut in the same shape : look in the dim light , like high-shouldered ghost with their hand in their breech pocket .
Coffin-plates , elm-chips , bright-headed nail , and shred of black cloth , lay scatter on the floor ; and the wall behind the counter be ornament with a lively representation of two mute in very stiff neckcloth , on duty at a large private door , with a hearse draw by four black steed , approach in the distance .
The shop be close and hot .
The atmosphere seem taint with the smell of coffin .
The recess beneath the counter in which his flock mattress be thrust , look like a grave .
Nor be these the only dismal feeling which depress Oliver .
He be alone in a strange place ; and we all know how chilled and desolate the best of u will sometimes feel in such a situation .
The boy have no friend to care for , or to care for him .
The regret of no recent separation be fresh in his mind ; the absence of no loved and well-remembered face sink heavily into his heart .
But his heart be heavy , notwithstanding ; and he wish , a he creep into his narrow bed , that that be his coffin , and that he could be lie in a calm and lasting sleep in the churchyard ground , with the tall grass wave gently above his head , and the sound of the old deep bell to soothe him in his sleep .
Oliver be awaken in the morning , by a loud kicking at the outside of the shop-door : which , before he could huddle on his clothes , be repeat , in an angry and impetuous manner , about twenty-five time .

CHAPTER VI .
OLIVER , BEING GOADED BY THE TAUNTS OF NOAH , ROUSES INTO ACTION , AND RATHER ASTONISHES HIM The month ’ s trial over , Oliver be formally apprentice .

The old inhabitant recollect no period at which measles have be so prevalent , or so fatal to infant existence ; and many be the mournful procession which little Oliver head , in a hat-band reach down to his knee , to the indescribable admiration and emotion of all the mother in the town .

For instance ; when Sowerberry have an order for the burial of some rich old lady or gentleman , who be surround by a great number of nephew and niece , who have be perfectly inconsolable during the previous illness , and whose grief have be wholly irrepressible even on the most public occasion , they would be a happy among themselves a need be—quite cheerful and contented—conversing together with as much freedom and gaiety , a if nothing whatever have happen to disturb them .

All this be very pleasant and improve to see ; and Oliver behold it with great admiration .
That Oliver Twist be move to resignation by the example of these good people , I can not , although I be his biographer , undertake to affirm with any degree of confidence ; but I can most distinctly say , that for many month he continue meekly to submit to the domination and ill-treatment of Noah Claypole : who use him far bad than before , now that his jealousy be rouse by see the new boy promote to the black stick and hatband , while he , the old one , remain stationary in the muffin-cap and leather .

And now , I come to a very important passage in Oliver ’ s history ; for I have to record an act , slight and unimportant perhaps in appearance , but which indirectly produce a material change in all his future prospect and proceeding .
One day , Oliver and Noah have descend into the kitchen at the usual dinner-hour , to banquet upon a small joint of mutton—a pound and a half of the bad end of the neck—when Charlotte be call out of the way , there ensue a brief interval of time , which Noah Claypole , be hungry and vicious , consider he could not possibly devote to a worthy purpose than aggravate and tantalise young Oliver Twist .

He get rather personal .

CHAPTER VII .
OLIVER CONTINUES REFRACTORY Noah Claypole run along the street at his swift pace , and pause not once for breath , until he reach the workhouse-gate .

He have listen to their taunt with a look of contempt ; he have bear the lash without a cry : for he felt that pride swell in his heart which would have keep down a shriek to the last , though they have roast him alive .

His way lay directly in front of the cottage .
His heart beat quickly when he bethink himself of this ; and he half resolve to turn back .

CHAPTER VIII .
OLIVER WALKS TO LONDON .
HE ENCOUNTERS ON THE ROAD A STRANGE SORT OF YOUNG GENTLEMAN Oliver reach the stile at which the by-path terminate ; and once more gain the high-road .
It be eight o ’ clock now .
Though he be nearly five mile away from the town , he run , and hid behind the hedge , by turn , till noon : fearing that he might be pursue and overtake .
Then he sit down to rest by the side of the milestone , and begin to think , for the first time , where he have well go and try to live .
The stone by which he be seat , bore , in large character , an intimation that it be just seventy mile from that spot to London .
The name awaken a new train of idea in the boy ’ s mind .
London ! —that great place ! —nobody—not even Mr. Bumble—could ever find him there !
He have often hear the old men in the workhouse , too , say that no lad of spirit need want in London ; and that there be way of living in that vast city , which those who have be breed up in country part have no idea of .
It be the very place for a homeless boy , who must die in the street unless some one help him .
As these thing pass through his thought , he jump upon his foot , and again walked forward .
He have diminish the distance between himself and London by full four mile more , before he recollect how much he must undergo ere he could hope to reach his place of destination .
As this consideration force itself upon him , he slacken his pace a little , and meditate upon his mean of get there .
He have a crust of bread , a coarse shirt , and two pair of stocking , in his bundle .
He have a penny too—a gift of Sowerberry ’ s after some funeral in which he have acquit himself more than ordinarily well—in his pocket .
“ A clean shirt , ” think Oliver , “ be a very comfortable thing ; and so be two pair of darned stocking ; and so be a penny ; but they be small help to a sixty-five mile ’ walk in winter time. ” But Oliver ’ s thought , like those of most other people , although they be extremely ready and active to point out his difficulty , be wholly at a loss to suggest any feasible mode of surmount them ; so , after a good deal of think to no particular purpose , he change his little bundle over to the other shoulder , and trudge on .
Oliver walk twenty mile that day ; and all that time taste nothing but the crust of dry bread , and a few draught of water , which he beg at the cottage-doors by the road-side .
When the night come , he turn into a meadow ; and , creep close under a hay-rick , determine to lie there , till morning .
He felt frighten at first , for the wind moan dismally over the empty field : and he be cold and hungry , and more alone than he have ever felt before .
Being very tire with his walk , however , he soon fell asleep and forget his trouble .
He felt cold and stiff , when he get up next morning , and so hungry that he be oblige to exchange the penny for a small loaf , in the very first village through which he pass .
He have walk no more than twelve mile , when night close in again .
His foot be sore , and his leg so weak that they tremble beneath him .
Another night pass in the bleak damp air , make him bad ; when he set forward on his journey next morning he could hardly crawl along .
He wait at the bottom of a steep hill till a stage-coach come up , and then beg of the outside passenger ; but there be very few who take any notice of him : and even those tell him to wait till they get to the top of the hill , and then let them see how far he could run for a halfpenny .
Poor Oliver try to keep up with the coach a little way , but be unable to do it , by reason of his fatigue and sore foot .
When the outside saw this , they put their halfpenny back into their pocket again , declare that he be an idle young dog , and didn ’ t deserve anything ; and the coach rattle away and leave only a cloud of dust behind .
In some village , large paint board be fix up : warn all person who beg within the district , that they would be send to jail .
This frighten Oliver very much , and make him glad to get out of those village with all possible expedition .
In others , he would stand about the inn-yards , and look mournfully at every one who pass : a proceeding which generally terminate in the landlady ’ s order one of the post-boys who be lounge about , to drive that strange boy out of the place , for she be sure he have come to steal something .
If he beg at a farmer ’ s house , ten to one but they threaten to set the dog on him ; and when he show his nose in a shop , they talk about the beadle—which bring Oliver ’ s heart into his mouth , —very often the only thing he have there , for many hour together .
In fact , if it have not be for a good-hearted turnpike-man , and a benevolent old lady , Oliver ’ s trouble would have be shorten by the very same process which have put an end to his mother ’ s ; in other word , he would most assuredly have fall dead upon the king ’ s highway .
But the turnpike-man give him a meal of bread and cheese ; and the old lady , who have a shipwrecked grandson wander barefoot in some distant part of the earth , take pity upon the poor orphan , and give him what little she could afford—and more—with such kind and gentle word , and such tear of sympathy and compassion , that they sink deeper into Oliver ’ s soul , than all the suffering he have ever undergone .
Early on the seventh morning after he have leave his native place , Oliver limp slowly into the little town of Barnet .
The window-shutters be close ; the street be empty ; not a soul have awaken to the business of the day .
The sun be rise in all it splendid beauty ; but the light only serve to show the boy his own lonesomeness and desolation , a he sit , with bleeding foot and cover with dust , upon a door-step .
By degree , the shutter be open ; the window-blinds be draw up ; and people begin pass to and fro .
Some few stop to gaze at Oliver for a moment or two , or turn round to stare at him a they hurry by ; but none relieve him , or trouble themselves to inquire how he come there .
He have no heart to beg .
And there he sit .
He have be crouch on the step for some time : wondering at the great number of public-houses ( every other house in Barnet be a tavern , large or small ) , gaze listlessly at the coach a they pass through , and think how strange it seem that they could do , with ease , in a few hour , what it have take him a whole week of courage and determination beyond his year to accomplish : when he be rouse by observe that a boy , who have pass him carelessly some minute before , have return , and be now survey him most earnestly from the opposite side of the way .
He take little heed of this at first ; but the boy remain in the same attitude of close observation so long , that Oliver raise his head , and return his steady look .
Upon this , the boy cross over ; and walk close up to Oliver , say , “ Hullo , my covey !
What ’ s the row ? ” The boy who address this inquiry to the young wayfarer , be about his own age : but one of the queer look boy that Oliver have even see .
He be a snub-nosed , flat-browed , common-faced boy enough ; and a dirty a juvenile a one would wish to see ; but he have about him all the air and manner of a man .
He be short of his age : with rather bow-legs , and little , sharp , ugly eye .
His hat be stick on the top of his head so lightly , that it threaten to fall off every moment—and would have do so , very often , if the wearer have not have a knack of every now and then give his head a sudden twitch , which bring it back to it old place again .
He wear a man ’ s coat , which reach nearly to his heel .
He have turn the cuff back , half-way up his arm , to get his hand out of the sleeve : apparently with the ultimate view of thrust them into the pocket of his corduroy trouser ; for there he keep them .
He be , altogether , a roystering and swagger a young gentleman a ever stand four foot six , or something less , in the blucher .

What ’ s the row ? ” say this strange young gentleman to Oliver .
“ I be very hungry and tired , ” reply Oliver : the tear stand in his eye a he speak .
“ I have walk a long way .
I have be walk these seven days. ” “ Walking for sivin day ! ” say the young gentleman .
“ Oh , I see .
Beak ’ s order , eh ?
But , ” he add , notice Oliver ’ s look of surprise , “ I suppose you don ’ t know what a beak be , my flash com-pan-i-on. ” Oliver mildly reply , that he have always hear a bird ’ s mouth describe by the term in question .

“ Yes. ” “ Got any lodging ? ” “ No. ” “ Money ? ” “ No. ” The strange boy whistle ; and put his arm into his pocket , as far a the big coat-sleeves would let them go .

“ Yes .
I do , when I ’ m at home , ” reply the boy .

Oh , no !

This unexpected offer of shelter be too tempting to be resist ; especially a it be immediately follow up , by the assurance that the old gentleman refer to , would doubtless provide Oliver with a comfortable place , without loss of time .
This lead to a more friendly and confidential dialogue ; from which Oliver discover that his friend ’ s name be Jack Dawkins , and that he be a peculiar pet and protege of the elderly gentleman before mention .
Mr. Dawkin ’ s appearance do not say a vast deal in favour of the comfort which his patron ’ s interest obtain for those whom he take under his protection ; but , a he have a rather flightly and dissolute mode of conversing , and furthermore avow that among his intimate friend he be well know by the sobriquet of “ The Artful Dodger , ” Oliver conclude that , be of a dissipated and careless turn , the moral precept of his benefactor have hitherto be throw away upon him .
Under this impression , he secretly resolve to cultivate the good opinion of the old gentleman as quickly a possible ; and , if he find the Dodger incorrigible , a he more than half suspect he should , to decline the honour of his farther acquaintance .
As John Dawkins object to their entering London before nightfall , it be nearly eleven o ’ clock when they reach the turnpike at Islington .
They cross from the Angel into St. John ’ s Road ; strike down the small street which terminate at Sadler ’ s Wells Theatre ; through Exmouth Street and Coppice Row ; down the little court by the side of the workhouse ; across the classic ground which once bore the name of Hockley-in-the-Hole ; thence into Little Saffron Hill ; and so into Saffron Hill the Great : along which the Dodger scud at a rapid pace , direct Oliver to follow close at his heel .
Although Oliver have enough to occupy his attention in keep sight of his leader , he could not help bestow a few hasty glance on either side of the way , a he pass along .
A dirtier or more wretched place he have never see .
The street be very narrow and muddy , and the air be impregnate with filthy odour .
There be a good many small shop ; but the only stock in trade appear to be heap of child , who , even at that time of night , be crawl in and out at the door , or scream from the inside .
The sole place that seem to prosper amid the general blight of the place , be the public-houses ; and in them , the low order of Irish be wrangle with might and main .

“ Plummy and slam ! ” be the reply .

Is Fagin upstairs ? ” “ Yes , he ’ s a sortin ’ the wipe .

Oliver , grope his way with one hand , and have the other firmly grasp by his companion , ascend with much difficulty the dark and broken stair : which his conductor mount with an ease and expedition that show he be well acquaint with them .
He throw open the door of a back-room , and draw Oliver in after him .
The wall and ceiling of the room be perfectly black with age and dirt .
There be a deal table before the fire : upon which be a candle , stick in a ginger-beer bottle , two or three pewter pot , a loaf and butter , and a plate .

These all crowd about their associate a he whisper a few word to the Jew ; and then turn round and grin at Oliver .
So do the Jew himself , toasting-fork in hand .

Upon this , the young gentleman with the pipe come round him , and shake both his hand very hard—especially the one in which he hold his little bundle .
One young gentleman be very anxious to hang up his cap for him ; and another be so obliging a to put his hand in his pocket , in order that , a he be very tired , he might not have the trouble of empty them , himself , when he go to bed .
These civility would probably be extend much far , but for a liberal exercise of the Jew ’ s toasting-fork on the head and shoulder of the affectionate youth who offer them .

In the midst of which they go to supper .
Oliver eat his share , and the Jew then mix him a glass of hot gin-and-water : telling him he must drink it off directly , because another gentleman want the tumbler .
Oliver do a he be desire .
Immediately afterwards he felt himself gently lift on to one of the sack ; and then he sink into a deep sleep .
CHAPTER IX .
CONTAINING FURTHER PARTICULARS CONCERNING THE PLEASANT OLD GENTLEMAN , AND HIS HOPEFUL PUPILS It be late next morning when Oliver awake , from a sound , long sleep .
There be no other person in the room but the old Jew , who be boil some coffee in a saucepan for breakfast , and whistle softly to himself a he stir it round and round , with an iron spoon .
He would stop every now and then to listen when there be the least noise below : and when he have satisfy himself , he would go on whistling and stirring again , a before .
Although Oliver have rouse himself from sleep , he be not thoroughly awake .
There be a drowsy state , between sleep and waking , when you dream more in five minute with your eye half open , and yourself half conscious of everything that be pass around you , than you would in five night with your eye fast close , and your sens wrapt in perfect unconsciousness .

CHAPTER X. OLIVER BECOMES BETTER ACQUAINTED WITH THE CHARACTERS OF HIS NEW ASSOCIATES ; AND PURCHASES EXPERIENCE AT A HIGH PRICE .
BEING A SHORT , BUT VERY IMPORTANT CHAPTER , IN THIS HISTORY For many day , Oliver remain in the Jew ’ s room , pick the mark out of the pocket-handkerchief , ( of which a great number be bring home , ) and sometimes take part in the game already describe : which the two boy and the Jew play , regularly , every morning .

CHAPTER XI .
TREATS OF MR. FANG THE POLICE MAGISTRATE ; AND FURNISHES A SLIGHT SPECIMEN OF HIS MODE OF ADMINISTERING JUSTICE The offence have be commit within the district , and indeed in the immediate neighborhood of , a very notorious metropolitan police office .

But this be little .
In our station-houses , men and woman be every night confine on the most trivial charges—the word be worth noting—in dungeon , compare with which , those in Newgate , occupy by the most atrocious felon , try , find guilty , and under sentence of death , be palace .

If I want any further proof of the strictly philosophical nature of the conduct of these young gentleman in their very delicate predicament , I should at once find it in the fact ( also record in a foregoing part of this narrative ) , of their quit the pursuit , when the general attention be fix upon Oliver ; and make immediately for their home by the short possible cut .
Although I do not mean to assert that it be usually the practice of renowned and learned sage , to shorten the road to any great conclusion ( their course indeed be rather to lengthen the distance , by various circumlocution and discursive staggerings , like unto those in which drunken men under the pressure of a too mighty flow of idea , be prone to indulge ) ; still , I do mean to say , and do say distinctly , that it be the invariable practice of many mighty philosopher , in carry out their theory , to evince great wisdom and foresight in provide against every possible contingency which can be suppose at all likely to affect themselves .
Thus , to do a great right , you may do a little wrong ; and you may take any mean which the end to be attain , will justify ; the amount of the right , or the amount of the wrong , or indeed the distinction between the two , be leave entirely to the philosopher concern , to be settle and determine by his clear , comprehensive , and impartial view of his own particular case .

CHAPTER XVII .
OLIVER ’ S DESTINY CONTINUING UNPROPITIOUS , BRINGS A GREAT MAN TO LONDON TO INJURE HIS REPUTATION It be the custom on the stage , in all good murderous melodrama , to present the tragic and the comic scene , in a regular alternation , a the layer of red and white in a side of streaky bacon .
The hero sink upon his straw bed , weigh down by fetter and misfortune ; in the next scene , his faithful but unconscious squire regale the audience with a comic song .
We behold , with throb bosom , the heroine in the grasp of a proud and ruthless baron : her virtue and her life alike in danger , draw forth her dagger to preserve the one at the cost of the other ; and just a our expectation be work up to the high pitch , a whistle be hear , and we be straightway transport to the great hall of the castle ; where a grey-headed seneschal sing a funny chorus with a funny body of vassal , who be free of all sort of place , from church vault to palace , and roam about in company , carol perpetually .
Such change appear absurd ; but they be not so unnatural a they would seem at first sight .
The transition in real life from well-spread board to death-beds , and from mourning-weeds to holiday garment , be not a whit less startling ; only , there , we be busy actor , instead of passive lookers-on , which make a vast difference .
The actor in the mimic life of the theatre , be blind to violent transition and abrupt impulse of passion or feeling , which , present before the eye of mere spectator , be at once condemn a outrageous and preposterous .
As sudden shifting of the scene , and rapid change of time and place , be not only sanction in book by long usage , but be by many consider a the great art of authorship : an author ’ s skill in his craft be , by such critic , chiefly estimate with relation to the dilemma in which he leave his character at the end of every chapter : this brief introduction to the present one may perhaps be deem unnecessary .
If so , let it be consider a delicate intimation on the part of the historian that he be go back to the town in which Oliver Twist be bear ; the reader take it for grant that there be good and substantial reason for make the journey , or he would not be invite to proceed upon such an expedition .

CHAPTER XVIII .
HOW OLIVER PASSED HIS TIME IN THE IMPROVING SOCIETY OF HIS REPUTABLE FRIENDS About noon next day , when the Dodger and Master Bates have go out to pursue their customary avocation , Mr. Fagin take the opportunity of read Oliver a long lecture on the cry sin of ingratitude ; of which he clearly demonstrate he have be guilty , to no ordinary extent , in wilfully absent himself from the society of his anxious friend ; and , still more , in endeavour to escape from them after so much trouble and expense have be incur in his recovery .
Mr. Fagin lay great stress on the fact of his have take Oliver in , and cherish him , when , without his timely aid , he might have perish with hunger ; and he relate the dismal and affect history of a young lad whom , in his philanthropy , he have succour under parallel circumstance , but who , prove unworthy of his confidence and evince a desire to communicate with the police , have unfortunately come to be hang at the Old Bailey one morning .
Mr. Fagin do not seek to conceal his share in the catastrophe , but lament with tear in his eye that the wrong-headed and treacherous behaviour of the young person in question , have render it necessary that he should become the victim of certain evidence for the crown : which , if it be not precisely true , be indispensably necessary for the safety of him ( Mr. Fagin ) and a few select friend .

Which , never fail to revert to his kind friend , and the opinion they must long ago have form of him , be sad indeed .

Oliver be but too glad to make himself useful ; too happy to have some face , however bad , to look upon ; too desirous to conciliate those about him when he could honestly do so ; to throw any objection in the way of this proposal .
So he at once express his readiness ; and , kneel on the floor , while the Dodger sit upon the table so that he could take his foot in his lap , he apply himself to a process which Mr. Dawkins designate a “ japan his trotter-cases. ” The phrase , render into plain English , signifieth , clean his boot .
Whether it be the sense of freedom and independence which a rational animal may be suppose to feel when he sit on a table in an easy attitude smoke a pipe , swing one leg carelessly to and fro , and have his boot clean all the time , without even the past trouble of have take them off , or the prospective misery of put them on , to disturb his reflection ; or whether it be the goodness of the tobacco that soothe the feeling of the Dodger , or the mildness of the beer that mollify his thought ; he be evidently tincture , for the nonce , with a spice of romance and enthusiasm , foreign to his general nature .
He look down on Oliver , with a thoughtful countenance , for a brief space ; and then , raise his head , and heave a gentle sign , say , half in abstraction , and half to Master Bates : “ What a pity it be he isn ’ t a prig ! ” “ Ah ! ” say Master Charles Bates ; “ he don ’ t know what ’ s good for him. ” The Dodger sigh again , and resume his pipe : a do Charley Bates .

“ So ’ s Charley .

CHAPTER XXI .
THE EXPEDITION It be a cheerless morning when they get into the street ; blowing and rain hard ; and the cloud look dull and stormy .
The night have be very wet : large pool of water have collect in the road : and the kennel be overflow .
There be a faint glimmering of the come day in the sky ; but it rather aggravate than relieve the gloom of the scene : the sombre light only serve to pale that which the street lamp afford , without shed any warmer or brighter tint upon the wet house-tops , and dreary street .
There appear to be nobody stir in that quarter of the town ; the window of the house be all closely shut ; and the street through which they pass , be noiseless and empty .
By the time they have turn into the Bethnal Green Road , the day have fairly begin to break .
Many of the lamp be already extinguish ; a few country waggon be slowly toil on , towards London ; now and then , a stage-coach , cover with mud , rattle briskly by : the driver bestowing , a he pass , an admonitory lash upon the heavy waggoner who , by keep on the wrong side of the road , have endanger his arriving at the office , a quarter of a minute after his time .
The public-houses , with gas-lights burn inside , be already open .
By degree , other shop begin to be unclosed , and a few scattered people be meet with .
Then , come straggle group of labourer go to their work ; then , men and woman with fish-baskets on their head ; donkey-carts lade with vegetable ; chaise-carts fill with live-stock or whole carcass of meat ; milk-women with pail ; an unbroken concourse of people , trudge out with various supply to the eastern suburb of the town .
As they approach the City , the noise and traffic gradually increase ; when they thread the street between Shoreditch and Smithfield , it have swell into a roar of sound and bustle .
It be as light a it be likely to be , till night come on again , and the busy morning of half the London population have begin .
Turning down Sun Street and Crown Street , and cross Finsbury square , Mr. Sikes strike , by way of Chiswell Street , into Barbican : thence into Long Lane , and so into Smithfield ; from which latter place arise a tumult of discordant sound that fill Oliver Twist with amazement .
It be market-morning .
The ground be cover , nearly ankle-deep , with filth and mire ; a thick steam , perpetually rise from the reek body of the cattle , and mingle with the fog , which seem to rest upon the chimney-tops , hang heavily above .
All the pen in the centre of the large area , and a many temporary pen a could be crowd into the vacant space , be fill with sheep ; tie up to post by the gutter side be long line of beast and ox , three or four deep .
Countrymen , butcher , drover , hawker , boy , thief , idler , and vagabond of every low grade , be mingle together in a mass ; the whistling of drover , the bark dog , the bellowing and plunging of the ox , the bleating of sheep , the grunting and squeaking of pig , the cry of hawker , the shout , oath , and quarrel on all side ; the ringing of bell and roar of voice , that issue from every public-house ; the crowding , push , drive , beating , whoop and yelling ; the hideous and discordant dim that resound from every corner of the market ; and the unwashed , unshaven , squalid , and dirty figure constantly run to and fro , and burst in and out of the throng ; render it a stunning and bewildering scene , which quite confound the sens .
Mr. Sikes , drag Oliver after him , elbow his way through the thickest of the crowd , and bestow very little attention on the numerous sight and sound , which so astonish the boy .
He nod , twice or thrice , to a passing friend ; and , resist a many invitation to take a morning dram , press steadily onward , until they be clear of the turmoil , and have make their way through Hosier Lane into Holborn .
“ Now , young ’ un ! ” say Sikes , look up at the clock of St. Andrew ’ s Church , “ hard upon seven !
you must step out .

In with you ! ” Thus address Oliver , he help him into the cart ; and the driver , point to a heap of sack , tell him to lie down there , and rest himself .

Kensington , Hammersmith , Chiswick , Kew Bridge , Brentford , be all pass ; and yet they go on as steadily a if they have only just begin their journey .
At length , they come to a public-house call the Coach and Horses ; a little way beyond which , another road appear to run off .

A young dog !

Sikes , with Oliver ’ s hand still in his , softly approach the low porch , and raise the latch .

CHAPTER XXIII .
WHICH CONTAINS THE SUBSTANCE OF A PLEASANT CONVERSATION BETWEEN MR. BUMBLE AND A LADY ; AND SHOWS THAT EVEN A BEADLE MAY BE SUSCEPTIBLE ON SOME POINTS The night be bitter cold .

CHAPTER XXIV .
TREATS ON A VERY POOR SUBJECT .
BUT IS A SHORT ONE , AND MAY BE FOUND OF IMPORTANCE IN THIS HISTORY It be no unfit messenger of death , who have disturb the quiet of the matron ’ s room .
Her body be bend by age ; her limb tremble with palsy ; her face , distort into a mumbling leer , resemble more the grotesque shaping of some wild pencil , than the work of Nature ’ s hand .
Alas !
How few of Nature ’ s face be leave alone to gladden u with their beauty !
The care , and sorrow , and hungerings , of the world , change them a they change heart ; and it be only when those passion sleep , and have lose their hold for ever , that the troubled cloud pass off , and leave Heaven ’ s surface clear .
It be a common thing for the countenance of the dead , even in that fixed and rigid state , to subside into the long-forgotten expression of sleep infancy , and settle into the very look of early life ; so calm , so peaceful , do they grow again , that those who know them in their happy childhood , kneel by the coffin ’ s side in awe , and see the Angel even upon earth .
The old crone totter along the passage , and up the stair , mutter some indistinct answer to the chiding of her companion ; be at length compel to pause for breath , she give the light into her hand , and remain behind to follow a she might : while the more nimble superior make her way to the room where the sick woman lay .

there , Mrs. Corney. ” “ It be , be it , sir ? ” ask the matron .

Is she doze , old lady ? ” The attendant stoop over the bed , to ascertain ; and nod in the affirmative .

“ His hand be not in .

Send him out with the Dodger and Charley ?

Fagin !

CHAPTER XXVII .
ATONES FOR THE UNPOLITENESS OF A FORMER CHAPTER ; WHICH DESERTED A LADY , MOST UNCEREMONIOUSLY As it would be , by no mean , seemly in a humble author to keep so mighty a personage a a beadle waiting , with his back to the fire , and the skirt of his coat gather up under his arm , until such time a it might suit his pleasure to relieve him ; and a it would still less become his station , or his gallantry to involve in the same neglect a lady on whom that beadle have look with an eye of tenderness and affection , and in whose ear he have whisper sweet word , which , come from such a quarter , might well thrill the bosom of maid or matron of whatsoever degree ; the historian whose pen trace these words—trusting that he know his place , and that he entertain a become reverence for those upon earth to whom high and important authority be delegated—hastens to pay them that respect which their position demand , and to treat them with all that duteous ceremony which their exalt rank , and ( by consequence ) great virtue , imperatively claim at his hand .
Towards this end , indeed , he have purpose to introduce , in this place , a dissertation touch the divine right of beadle , and elucidative of the position , that a beadle can do no wrong : which could not fail to have be both pleasurable and profitable to the right-minded reader but which he be unfortunately compel , by want of time and space , to postpone to some more convenient and fitting opportunity ; on the arrival of which , he will be prepare to show , that a beadle properly constitute : that be to say , a parochial beadle , attach to a parochial workhouse , and attend in his official capacity the parochial church : be , in right and virtue of his office , possess of all the excellence and best quality of humanity ; and that to none of those excellence , can mere company ’ beadle , or court-of-law beadle , or even chapel-of-ease beadle ( save the last , and they in a very lowly and inferior degree ) , lay the remote sustainable claim .

CHAPTER XXVIII .
LOOKS AFTER OLIVER , AND PROCEEDS WITH HIS ADVENTURES “ Wolves tear your throat ! ” mutter Sikes , grind his teeth .
“ I wish I be among some of you ; you ’ d howl the hoarser for it. ” As Sikes growl forth this imprecation , with the most desperate ferocity that his desperate nature be capable of , he rest the body of the wounded boy across his bended knee ; and turn his head , for an instant , to look back at his pursuer .
There be little to be make out , in the mist and darkness ; but the loud shouting of men vibrate through the air , and the barking of the neighbouring dog , rouse by the sound of the alarm bell , resound in every direction .
“ Stop , you white-livered hound ! ” cry the robber , shout after Toby Crackit , who , make the best use of his long leg , be already ahead .

Oh !
if when we oppress and grind our fellow-creatures , we bestow but one thought on the dark evidence of human error , which , like dense and heavy cloud , be rise , slowly it be true , but not less surely , to Heaven , to pour their after-vengeance on our head ; if we hear but one instant , in imagination , the deep testimony of dead men ’ s voice , which no power can stifle , and no pride shut out ; where would be the injury and injustice , the suffering , misery , cruelty , and wrong , that each day ’ s life bring with it !

Meanwhile , Oliver gradually thrive and prosper under the united care of Mrs. Maylie , Rose , and the kind-hearted Mr. Losberne .
If fervent prayer , gush from heart overcharge with gratitude , be hear in heaven—and if they be not , what prayer be ! —the blessing which the orphan child call down upon them , sink into their soul , diffuse peace and happiness .
CHAPTER XXXII .

Who can describe the pleasure and delight , the peace of mind and soft tranquillity , the sickly boy felt in the balmy air , and among the green hill and rich wood , of an inland village !
Who can tell how scene of peace and quietude sink into the mind of pain-worn dweller in close and noisy place , and carry their own freshness , deep into their jaded heart !
Men who have live in crowded , pent-up street , through life of toil , and who have never wish for change ; men , to whom custom have indeed be second nature , and who have come almost to love each brick and stone that form the narrow boundary of their daily walk ; even they , with the hand of death upon them , have be know to yearn at last for one short glimpse of Nature ’ s face ; and , carry far from the scene of their old pain and pleasure , have seem to pass at once into a new state of be .

It be a lovely spot to which they repair .

Hard by , be a little churchyard ; not crowd with tall unsightly gravestone , but full of humble mound , cover with fresh turf and moss : beneath which , the old people of the village lay at rest .
Oliver often wander here ; and , think of the wretched grave in which his mother lay , would sometimes sit him down and sob unseen ; but , when he raise his eye to the deep sky overhead , he would cease to think of her a lie in the ground , and would weep for her , sadly , but without pain .
It be a happy time .
The day be peaceful and serene ; the night bring with them neither fear nor care ; no languishing in a wretched prison , or associate with wretched men ; nothing but pleasant and happy thought .

Then , he would walk with Mrs. Maylie and Rose , and hear them talk of book ; or perhaps sit near them , in some shady place , and listen whilst the young lady read : which he could have do , until it grow too dark to see the letter .
Then , he have his own lesson for the next day to prepare ; and at this , he would work hard , in a little room which look into the garden , till even come slowly on , when the lady would walk out again , and he with them : listen with such pleasure to all they say : and so happy if they want a flower that he could climb to reach , or have forget anything he could run to fetch : that he could never be quick enough about it .

There would be no candle light at such time a these ; and Oliver would sit by one of the window , listen to the sweet music , in a perfect rapture .
And when Sunday come , how differently the day be spend , from any way in which he have ever spend it yet !
and how happily too ; like all the other day in that most happy time !
There be the little church , in the morning , with the green leave flutter at the window : the bird sing without : and the sweet-smelling air steal in at the low porch , and fill the homely building with it fragrance .
The poor people be so neat and clean , and kneel so reverently in prayer , that it seem a pleasure , not a tedious duty , their assembling there together ; and though the singing might be rude , it be real , and sound more musical ( to Oliver ’ s ear at least ) than any he have ever hear in church before .
Then , there be the walk a usual , and many call at the clean house of the labour men ; and at night , Oliver read a chapter or two from the Bible , which he have be study all the week , and in the performance of which duty he felt more proud and please , than if he have be the clergyman himself .
In the morning , Oliver would be a-foot by six o ’ clock , roam the field , and plunder the hedge , far and wide , for nosegay of wild flower , with which he would return laden , home ; and which it take great care and consideration to arrange , to the best advantage , for the embellishment of the breakfast-table .
There be fresh groundsel , too , for Miss Maylie ’ s bird , with which Oliver , who have be study the subject under the able tuition of the village clerk , would decorate the cage , in the most approved taste .

So three month glide away ; three month which , in the life of the most blessed and favour of mortal , might have be unmingled happiness , and which , in Oliver ’ s be true felicity .
With the purest and most amiable generosity on one side ; and the truest , warm , soul-felt gratitude on the other ; it be no wonder that , by the end of that short time , Oliver Twist have become completely domesticate with the old lady and her niece , and that the fervent attachment of his young and sensitive heart , be repay by their pride in , and attachment to , himself .
CHAPTER XXXIII .
WHEREIN THE HAPPINESS OF OLIVER AND HIS FRIENDS , EXPERIENCES A SUDDEN CHECK Spring fly swiftly by , and summer come .

“ Who would have think it !
Grind him to ash !

Oh !

Oh !

Again !

IS A VERY SHORT ONE , AND MAY APPEAR OF NO GREAT IMPORTANCE IN ITS PLACE , BUT IT SHOULD BE READ NOTWITHSTANDING , AS A SEQUEL TO THE LAST , AND A KEY TO ONE THAT WILL FOLLOW WHEN ITS TIME ARRIVES “ And so you be resolve to be my travel companion this morning ; eh ? ” say the doctor , a Harry Maylie join him and Oliver at the breakfast-table .

CHAPTER XXXVII .
IN WHICH THE READER MAY PERCEIVE A CONTRAST , NOT UNCOMMON IN MATRIMONIAL CASES Mr. Bumble sit in the workhouse parlour , with his eye moodily fix on the cheerless grate , whence , a it be summer time , no brighter gleam proceed , than the reflection of certain sickly ray of the sun , which be send back from it cold and shin surface .
A paper fly-cage dangle from the ceiling , to which he occasionally raise his eye in gloomy thought ; and , a the heedless insect hover round the gaudy net-work , Mr. Bumble would heave a deep sigh , while a more gloomy shadow overspread his countenance .
Mr. Bumble be meditate ; it might be that the insect bring to mind , some painful passage in his own past life .
Nor be Mr. Bumble ’ s gloom the only thing calculate to awaken a pleasing melancholy in the bosom of a spectator .
There be not want other appearance , and those closely connect with his own person , which announce that a great change have take place in the position of his affair .
The laced coat , and the cocked hat ; where be they ?
He still wear knee-breeches , and dark cotton stocking on his nether limb ; but they be not _the_ breech .
The coat be wide-skirted ; and in that respect like _the_ coat , but , oh how different !
The mighty cock hat be replace by a modest round one .
Mr. Bumble be no long a beadle .
There be some promotion in life , which , independent of the more substantial reward they offer , require peculiar value and dignity from the coat and waistcoat connect with them .
A field-marshal have his uniform ; a bishop his silk apron ; a counsellor his silk gown ; a beadle his cock hat .
Strip the bishop of his apron , or the beadle of his hat and lace ; what be they ?
Men .
Mere men .
Dignity , and even holiness too , sometimes , be more question of coat and waistcoat than some people imagine .
Mr. Bumble have marry Mrs. Corney , and be master of the workhouse .
Another beadle have come into power .
On him the cocked hat , gold-laced coat , and staff , have all three descend .
“ And to-morrow two month it be do ! ” say Mr. Bumble , with a sigh .
“ It seem a age. ” Mr. Bumble might have mean that he have concentrate a whole existence of happiness into the short space of eight week ; but the sigh—there be a vast deal of meaning in the sigh .
“ I sell myself , ” say Mr. Bumble , pursue the same train of relection , “ for six teaspoon , a pair of sugar-tongs , and a milk-pot ; with a small quantity of second-hand furniture , and twenty pound in money .
I go very reasonable .
Cheap , dirt cheap ! ” “ Cheap ! ” cry a shrill voice in Mr. Bumble ’ s ear : “ you would have be dear at any price ; and dear enough I pay for you , Lord above know that ! ” Mr. Bumble turn , and encounter the face of his interesting consort , who , imperfectly comprehend the few word she have overheard of his complaint , have hazard the forego remark at a venture .
“ Mrs .
Bumble , ma ’ be ! ” say Mr. Bumble , with a sentimental sternness .

Young Twist ! ” say Mr. Bumble ; “ I remember him , of course .

CHAPTER XXXVIII .
