CHAPTER XL .
A STRANGE INTERVIEW , WHICH IS A SEQUEL TO THE LAST CHAMBER The girl ’ s life have be squander in the street , and among the most noisome of the stew and den of London , but there be something of the woman ’ s original nature leave in her still ; and when she hear a light step approach the door opposite to that by which she have enter , and thought of the wide contrast which the small room would in another moment contain , she felt burden with the sense of her own deep shame , and shrunk a though she could scarcely bear the presence of her with whom she have seek this interview .
But struggle with these good feeling be pride , —the vice of the low and most debased creature no less than of the high and self-assured .
The miserable companion of thief and ruffian , the fall outcast of low haunt , the associate of the scouring of the jail and hulk , live within the shadow of the gallows itself , —even this degrade be felt too proud to betray a feeble gleam of the womanly feeling which she think a weakness , but which alone connect her with that humanity , of which her waste life have obliterate so many , many trace when a very child .

I be the person you inquire for. ” The kind tone of this answer , the sweet voice , the gentle manner , the absence of any accent of haughtiness or displeasure , take the girl completely by surprise , and she burst into tear .

CHAPTER XLVI .
THE APPOINTMENT KEPT The church clock chime three quarter past eleven , a two figure emerge on London Bridge .
One , which advance with a swift and rapid step , be that of a woman who look eagerly about her a though in quest of some expected object ; the other figure be that of a man , who slink along in the deep shadow he could find , and , at some distance , accommodate his pace to hers : stopping when she stop : and a she move again , creep stealthily on : but never allow himself , in the ardour of his pursuit , to gain upon her footstep .

It be a very dark night .
The day have be unfavourable , and at that hour and place there be few people stir .
Such a there be , hurry quickly past : very possibly without see , but certainly without notice , either the woman , or the man who keep her in view .
Their appearance be not calculate to attract the importunate regard of such of London ’ s destitute population , a chance to take their way over the bridge that night in search of some cold arch or doorless hovel wherein to lay their head ; they stand there in silence : neither speak nor spoken to , by any one who pass .
A mist hung over the river , deepen the red glare of the fire that burn upon the small craft moor off the different wharf , and render darker and more indistinct the murky building on the bank .
The old smoke-stained storehouse on either side , rise heavy and dull from the dense mass of roof and gable , and frown sternly upon water too black to reflect even their lumbering shape .
The tower of old Saint Saviour ’ s Church , and the spire of Saint Magnus , so long the giant-warders of the ancient bridge , be visible in the gloom ; but the forest of ship below bridge , and the thickly scatter spire of church above , be nearly all hidden from sight .
The girl have take a few restless turn to and fro—closely watch meanwhile by her hidden observer—when the heavy bell of St. Paul ’ s toll for the death of another day .
Midnight have come upon the crowded city .
The palace , the night-cellar , the jail , the madhouse : the chamber of birth and death , of health and sickness , the rigid face of the corpse and the calm sleep of the child : midnight be upon them all .

At this point the low step widen : so that a person turn that angle of the wall , be necessarily unseen by any others on the stair who chance to be above him , if only a step .

CHAPTER XLVII .
FATAL CONSEQUENCES It be nearly two hour before day-break ; that time which in the autumn of the year , may be truly call the dead of night ; when the street be silent and desert ; when even sound appear to slumber , and profligacy and riot have stagger home to dream ; it be at this still and silent hour , that Fagin sit watch in his old lair , with face so distorted and pale , and eye so red and blood-shot , that he look less like a man , than like some hideous phantom , moist from the grave , and worry by an evil spirit .
He sit crouch over a cold hearth , wrap in an old torn coverlet , with his face turn towards a waste candle that stand upon a table by his side .
His right hand be raise to his lip , and a , absorb in thought , he hit his long black nail , he disclose among his toothless gum a few such fang a should have be a dog ’ s or rat ’ s .
Stretched upon a mattress on the floor , lay Noah Claypole , fast asleep .
Towards him the old man sometimes direct his eye for an instant , and then bring them back again to the candle ; which with a long-burnt wick droop almost double , and hot grease fall down in clot upon the table , plainly show that his thought be busy elsewhere .
Indeed they be .
Mortification at the overthrow of his notable scheme ; hatred of the girl who have dare to palter with stranger ; and utter distrust of the sincerity of her refusal to yield him up ; bitter disappointment at the loss of his revenge on Sikes ; the fear of detection , and ruin , and death ; and a fierce and deadly rage kindle by all ; these be the passionate consideration which , follow close upon each other with rapid and ceaseless whirl , shot through the brain of Fagin , a every evil thought and black purpose lay work at his heart .
He sit without change his attitude in the least , or appear to take the small heed of time , until his quick ear seem to be attract by a footstep in the street .
“ At last , ” he mutter , wipe his dry and fevered mouth .
“ At last ! ” The bell ring gently a he speak .

It ’ s be trouble enough to get ; I think I should have be here , three hour ago. ” Fagin lay his hand upon the bundle , and lock it in the cupboard , sit down again without speaking .

The certainty of immediate detection if he fire , flash across his mind even in the midst of his fury ; and he beat it twice with all the force he could summon , upon the upturned face that almost touch his own .
She stagger and fell : nearly blind with the blood that rain down from a deep gash in her forehead ; but raise herself , with difficulty , on her knee , draw from her bosom a white handkerchief—Rose Maylie ’ s own—and hold it up , in her folded hand , a high towards Heaven a her feeble strength would allow , breathe one prayer for mercy to her Maker .
It be a ghastly figure to look upon .
The murderer stagger backward to the wall , and shut out the sight with his hand , seize a heavy club and strike her down .
CHAPTER XLVIII .
THE FLIGHT OF SIKES Of all bad deed that , under cover of the darkness , have be commit within wide London ’ s bound since night hung over it , that be the bad .
Of all the horror that rise with an ill scent upon the morning air , that be the foul and most cruel .
The sun—the bright sun , that bring back , not light alone , but new life , and hope , and freshness to man—burst upon the crowded city in clear and radiant glory .
Through costly-coloured glass and paper-mended window , through cathedral dome and rotten crevice , it shed it equal ray .
It light up the room where the murder woman lay .
It do .
He try to shut it out , but it would stream in .
If the sight have be a ghastly one in the dull morning , what be it , now , in all that brilliant light !
He have not move ; he have be afraid to stir .
There have be a moan and motion of the hand ; and , with terror add to rage , he have strike and strike again .
Once he throw a rug over it ; but it be bad to fancy the eye , and imagine them move towards him , than to see them glare upward , a if watch the reflection of the pool of gore that quiver and dance in the sunlight on the ceiling .
He have pluck it off again .
And there be the body—mere flesh and blood , no more—but such flesh , and so much blood !
He strike a light , kindle a fire , and thrust the club into it .
There be hair upon the end , which blaze and shrink into a light cinder , and , catch by the air , whirl up the chimney .
Even that frighten him , sturdy a he be ; but he hold the weapon till it break , and then pile it on the coal to burn away , and smoulder into ash .
He wash himself , and rub his clothes ; there be spot that would not be remove , but he cut the piece out , and burn them .
How those stain be disperse about the room !
The very foot of the dog be bloody .
All this time he have , never once , turn his back upon the corpse ; no , not for a moment .
Such preparation complete , he move , backward , towards the door : drag the dog with him , lest he should soil his foot anew and carry out new evidence of the crime into the street .
He shut the door softly , lock it , take the key , and leave the house .
He cross over , and glance up at the window , to be sure that nothing be visible from the outside .
There be the curtain still draw , which she would have open to admit the light she never saw again .
It lay nearly under there .
_He_ know that .
God , how the sun pour down upon the very spot !
The glance be instantaneous .
It be a relief to have get free of the room .
He whistle on the dog , and walk rapidly away .
He go through Islington ; stride up the hill at Highgate on which stand the stone in honour of Whittington ; turn down to Highgate Hill , unsteady of purpose , and uncertain where to go ; strike off to the right again , almost as soon a he begin to descend it ; and take the foot-path across the field , skirt Caen Wood , and so come on Hampstead Heath .
Traversing the hollow by the Vale of Heath , he mount the opposite bank , and cross the road which join the village of Hampstead and Highgate , make along the remain portion of the heath to the field at North End , in one of which he lay himself down under a hedge , and slept .
Soon he be up again , and away , —not far into the country , but back towards London by the high-road—then back again—then over another part of the same ground a he already traversed—then wander up and down in field , and lie on ditch ’ brink to rest , and start up to make for some other spot , and do the same , and ramble on again .
Where could he go , that be near and not too public , to get some meat and drink ?
Hendon .
That be a good place , not far off , and out of most people ’ s way .
Thither he direct his step , —running sometimes , and sometimes , with a strange perversity , loiter at a snail ’ s pace , or stop altogether and idly break the hedge with a stick .
But when he get there , all the people he met—the very child at the doors—seemed to view him with suspicion .
Back he turn again , without the courage to purchase bit or drop , though he have taste no food for many hour ; and once more he linger on the Heath , uncertain where to go .
He wander over mile and mile of ground , and still come back to the old place .
Morning and noon have pass , and the day be on the wane , and still he ramble to and fro , and up and down , and round and round , and still linger about the same spot .
At last he get away , and shape his course for Hatfield .
It be nine o ’ clock at night , when the man , quite tire out , and the dog , limp and lame from the unaccustomed exercise , turn down the hill by the church of the quiet village , and plod along the little street , creep into a small public-house , whose scanty light have guide them to the spot .
There be a fire in the tap-room , and some country-labourers be drink before it .
They make room for the stranger , but he sit down in the furthest corner , and ate and drink alone , or rather with his dog : to whom he cast a morsel of food from time to time .
The conversation of the men assemble here , turn upon the neighbouring land , and farmer ; and when those topic be exhaust , upon the age of some old man who have be bury on the previous Sunday ; the young men present consider him very old , and the old men present declare him to have be quite young—not old , one white-haired grandfather say , than he was—with ten or fifteen year of life in him at least—if he have take care ; if he have take care .
There be nothing to attract attention , or excite alarm in this .
The robber , after pay his reckoning , sit silent and unnoticed in his corner , and have almost drop asleep , when he be half waken by the noisy entrance of a new comer .
This be an antic fellow , half pedlar and half mountebank , who travel about the country on foot to vend hone , strop , razor , washballs , harness-paste , medicine for dog and horse , cheap perfumery , cosmetic , and such-like ware , which he carry in a case slung to his back .
His entrance be the signal for various homely joke with the countryman , which slacken not until he have make his supper , and open his box of treasure , when he ingeniously contrive to unite business with amusement .
“ And what be that stoof ?
Good to eat , Harry ? ” ask a grinning countryman , point to some composition-cakes in one corner .
“ This , ” say the fellow , produce one , “ this be the infallible and invaluable composition for remove all sort of stain , rust , dirt , mildew , spick , speck , spot , or spatter , from silk , satin , linen , cambric , cloth , crape , stuff , carpet , merino , muslin , bombazeen , or woollen stuff .
Wine-stains , fruit-stains , beer-stains , water-stains , paint-stains , pitch-stains , any stain , all come out at one rub with the infallible and invaluable composition .
If a lady stain her honour , she have only need to swallow one cake and she ’ s cure at once—for it ’ s poison .
If a gentleman want to prove this , he have only need to bolt one little square , and he have put it beyond question—for it ’ s quite as satisfactory a a pistol-bullet , and a great deal nastier in the flavour , consequently the more credit in take it .
One penny a square .
With all these virtue , one penny a square ! ” There be two buyer directly , and more of the listener plainly hesitate .
The vendor observe this , increase in loquacity .
“ It ’ s all buy up as fast a it can be make , ” say the fellow .
“ There be fourteen water-mills , six steam-engines , and a galvanic battery , always a-working upon it , and they can ’ t make it fast enough , though the men work so hard that they die off , and the widow be pension directly , with twenty pound a-year for each of the child , and a premium of fifty for twin .
One penny a square !
Two half-pence be all the same , and four farthing be receive with joy .
One penny a square !
Wine-stains , fruit-stains , beer-stains , water-stains , paint-stains , pitch-stains , mud-stains , blood-stains !
Here be a stain upon the hat of a gentleman in company , that I ’ ll take clean out , before he can order me a pint of ale. ” “ Hah ! ” cry Sikes start up .
“ Give that back. ” “ I ’ ll take it clean out , sir , ” reply the man , wink to the company , “ before you can come across the room to get it .
Gentlemen all , observe the dark stain upon this gentleman ’ s hat , no wider than a shilling , but thick than a half-crown .
Whether it be a wine-stain , fruit-stain , beer-stain , water-stain , paint-stain , pitch-stain , mud-stain , or blood-stain— ” The man get no further , for Sikes with a hideous imprecation overthrow the table , and tear the hat from him , burst out of the house .

He almost know what be to come ; but he cross over , and listen .
The guard be stand at the door , wait for the letter-bag .
A man , dress like a game-keeper , come up at the moment , and he hand him a basket which lay ready on the pavement .
“ That ’ s for your people , ” say the guard .
“ Now , look alive in there , will you .
Damn that ’ ere bag , it warn ’ t ready night afore last ; this win ’ t do , you know ! ” “ Anything new up in town , Ben ? ” ask the game-keeper , draw back to the window-shutters , the good to admire the horse .
“ No , nothing that I know on , ” reply the man , pull on his glove .
“ Corn ’ s up a little .
I heerd talk of a murder , too , down Spitalfields way , but I don ’ t reckon much upon it. ” “ Oh , that ’ s quite true , ” say a gentleman inside , who be look out of the window .
“ And a dreadful murder it was. ” “ Was it , sir ? ” rejoin the guard , touch his hat .
“ Man or woman , pray , sir ? ” “ A woman , ” reply the gentleman .
“ It be supposed— ” “ Now , Ben , ” reply the coachman impatiently .
“ Damn that ’ ere bag , ” say the guard ; “ be you go to sleep in there ? ” “ Coming ! ” cry the office keeper , run out .
“ Coming , ” growl the guard .
“ Ah , and so ’ s the young ’ ooman of property that ’ s go to take a fancy to me , but I don ’ t know when .
Here , give hold .
All ri—ight ! ” The horn sound a few cheerful note , and the coach be go .
Sikes remain stand in the street , apparently unmoved by what he have just hear , and agitate by no strong feeling than a doubt where to go .
At length he go back again , and take the road which lead from Hatfield to St. Albans .
He go on doggedly ; but a he leave the town behind him , and plunge into the solitude and darkness of the road , he felt a dread and awe creep upon him which shake him to the core .
Every object before him , substance or shadow , still or move , take the semblance of some fearful thing ; but these fear be nothing compare to the sense that haunt him of that morning ’ s ghastly figure follow at his heel .
He could trace it shadow in the gloom , supply the small item of the outline , and note how stiff and solemn it seem to stalk along .
He could hear it garment rustle in the leaf , and every breath of wind come laden with that last low cry .
If he stop it do the same .
If he run , it followed—not run too : that would have be a relief : but like a corpse endow with the mere machinery of life , and borne on one slow melancholy wind that never rise or fell .
At time , he turn , with desperate determination , resolve to beat this phantom off , though it should look him dead ; but the hair rise on his head , and his blood stand still , for it have turn with him and be behind him then .
He have keep it before him that morning , but it be behind now—always .
He lean his back against a bank , and felt that it stand above him , visibly out against the cold night-sky .
He throw himself upon the road—on his back upon the road .
At his head it stand , silent , erect , and still—a living grave-stone , with it epitaph in blood .
Let no man talk of murderer escape justice , and hint that Providence must sleep .
There be twenty score of violent death in one long minute of that agony of fear .
There be a shed in a field he pass , that offer shelter for the night .
Before the door , be three tall poplar tree , which make it very dark within ; and the wind moan through them with a dismal wail .
He _could not_ walk on , till daylight come again ; and here he stretch himself close to the wall—to undergo new torture .
For now , a vision come before him , a constant and more terrible than that from which he have escape .
Those widely star eye , so lustreless and so glassy , that he have well borne to see them than think upon them , appear in the midst of the darkness : light in themselves , but give light to nothing .
There be but two , but they be everywhere .
If he shut out the sight , there come the room with every well-known object—some , indeed , that he would have forget , if he have go over it content from memory—each in it accustomed place .
The body be in _its_ place , and it eye be a he saw them when he steal away .
He get up , and rush into the field without .
The figure be behind him .
He re-enter the shed , and shrink down once more .
The eye be there , before he have lay himself along .
And here he remain in such terror a none but he can know , tremble in every limb , and the cold sweat start from every pore , when suddenly there arose upon the night-wind the noise of distant shouting , and the roar of voice mingle in alarm and wonder .
Any sound of men in that lonely place , even though it convey a real cause of alarm , be something to him .
He regain his strength and energy at the prospect of personal danger ; and spring to his foot , rush into the open air .
The broad sky seem on fire .
Rising into the air with shower of spark , and roll one above the other , be sheet of flame , light the atmosphere for mile round , and drive cloud of smoke in the direction where he stand .
The shout grow louder a new voice swell the roar , and he could hear the cry of Fire !
mingle with the ringing of an alarm-bell , the fall of heavy body , and the crackling of flame a they twin round some new obstacle , and shot aloft a though refresh by food .
The noise increase a he look .
There be people there—men and women—light , bustle .
It be like new life to him .
He dart onward—straight , headlong—dashing through brier and brake , and leap gate and fence as madly a his dog , who career with loud and sound bark before him .
He come upon the spot .
There be half-dressed figure tear to and fro , some endeavour to drag the frightened horse from the stable , others drive the cattle from the yard and out-houses , and others come laden from the burn pile , amidst a shower of fall spark , and the tumble down of red-hot beam .
The aperture , where door and window stand an hour ago , disclose a mass of rag fire ; wall rock and crumble into the burning well ; the molten lead and iron pour down , white hot , upon the ground .
Women and child shriek , and men encourage each other with noisy shout and cheer .
The clanking of the engine-pumps , and the spirting and hissing of the water a it fell upon the blazing wood , add to the tremendous roar .
He shout , too , till he be hoarse ; and fly from memory and himself , plunge into the thickest of the throng .
Hither and thither he dive that night : now work at the pump , and now hurry through the smoke and flame , but never cease to engage himself wherever noise and men be thick .
Up and down the ladder , upon the roof of building , over floor that quake and tremble with his weight , under the lee of fall brick and stone , in every part of that great fire be he ; but he bore a charmed life , and have neither scratch nor bruise , nor weariness nor thought , till morning dawn again , and only smoke and blacken ruin remain .
This mad excitement over , there return , with ten-fold force , the dreadful consciousness of his crime .
He look suspiciously about him , for the men be converse in group , and he fear to be the subject of their talk .
The dog obey the significant beck of his finger , and they draw off , stealthily , together .
He pass near an engine where some men be seat , and they call to him to share in their refreshment .
He take some bread and meat ; and a he drink a draught of beer , hear the fireman , who be from London , talk about the murder .
“ He have go to Birmingham , they say , ” say one : “ but they ’ ll have him yet , for the scout be out , and by to-morrow night there ’ ll be a cry all through the country. ” He hurry off , and walk till he almost drop upon the ground ; then lay down in a lane , and have a long , but broken and uneasy sleep .
He wander on again , irresolute and undecided , and oppress with the fear of another solitary night .
Suddenly , he take the desperate resolution to go back to London .
“ There ’ s somebody to speak to there , at all event , ” he think .
“ A good hiding-place , too .
They ’ ll never expect to nab me there , after this country scent .
Why can ’ t I lie by for a week or so , and , force blunt from Fagin , get abroad to France ?
Damme , I ’ ll risk it. ” He act upon this impulse without delay , and choose the least frequented road begin his journey back , resolve to lie conceal within a short distance of the metropolis , and , enter it at dusk by a circuitous route , to proceed straight to that part of it which he have fix on for his destination .
The dog , though .
If any description of him be out , it would not be forget that the dog be miss , and have probably go with him .
This might lead to his apprehension a he pass along the street .
He resolve to drown him , and walk on , look about for a pond : pick up a heavy stone and tie it to his handkerchief a he go .
The animal look up into his master ’ s face while these preparation be make ; whether his instinct apprehend something of their purpose , or the robber ’ s sidelong look at him be stern than ordinary , he skulk a little farther in the rear than usual , and cower a he come more slowly along .
When his master halt at the brink of a pool , and look round to call him , he stop outright .
“ Do you hear me call ?
Come here ! ” cry Sikes .
The animal come up from the very force of habit ; but a Sikes stoop to attach the handkerchief to his throat , he utter a low growl and start back .
“ Come back ! ” say the robber .
The dog wag his tail , but move not .
Sikes make a running noose and call him again .
The dog advance , retreat , pause an instant , and scour away at his hard speed .
The man whistle again and again , and sit down and wait in the expectation that he would return .
But no dog appear , and at length he resume his journey .
CHAPTER XLIX .
MONKS AND MR. BROWNLOW AT LENGTH MEET .
THEIR CONVERSATION , AND THE INTELLIGENCE THAT INTERRUPTS IT The twilight be begin to close in , when Mr. Brownlow alight from a hackney-coach at his own door , and knock softly .
The door be open , a sturdy man get out of the coach and station himself on one side of the step , while another man , who have be seat on the box , dismount too , and stand upon the other side .
At a sign from Mr. Brownlow , they help out a third man , and take him between them , hurry him into the house .
This man be Monks .
They walk in the same manner up the stair without speaking , and Mr. Brownlow , precede them , lead the way into a back-room .
At the door of this apartment , Monks , who have ascend with evident reluctance , stop .
The two men look at the old gentleman a if for instruction .
“ He know the alternative , ” say Mr. Browlow .
“ If he hesitate or move a finger but a you bid him , drag him into the street , call for the aid of the police , and impeach him a a felon in my name. ” “ How dare you say this of me ? ” ask Monks .
“ How dare you urge me to it , young man ? ” reply Mr. Brownlow , confront him with a steady look .
“ Are you mad enough to leave this house ?
Unhand him .
There , sir .
You be free to go , and we to follow .
But I warn you , by all I hold most solemn and most sacred , that instant will have you apprehend on a charge of fraud and robbery .
I be resolute and immoveable .
If you be determine to be the same , your blood be upon your own head ! ” “ By what authority be I kidnap in the street , and bring here by these dog ? ” ask Monks , look from one to the other of the men who stand beside him .
“ By mine , ” reply Mr. Brownlow .
“ Those person be indemnify by me .
If you complain of be deprive of your liberty—you have power and opportunity to retrieve it a you come along , but you deem it advisable to remain quiet—I say again , throw yourself for protection on the law .
I will appeal to the law too ; but when you have go too far to recede , do not sue to me for leniency , when the power will have pass into other hand ; and do not say I plunge you down the gulf into which you rush , yourself. ” Monks be plainly disconcert , and alarm besides .
He hesitate .
“ You will decide quickly , ” say Mr. Brownlow , with perfect firmness and composure .
“ If you wish me to prefer my charge publicly , and consign you to a punishment the extent of which , although I can , with a shudder , foresee , I can not control , once more , I say , for you know the way .
If not , and you appeal to my forbearance , and the mercy of those you have deeply injure , seat yourself , without a word , in that chair .
It have wait for you two whole days. ” Monks mutter some unintelligible word , but waver still .
“ You will be prompt , ” say Mr. Brownlow .
“ A word from me , and the alternative have go for ever. ” Still the man hesitate .
“ I have not the inclination to parley , ” say Mr. Brownlow , “ and , a I advocate the dear interest of others , I have not the right. ” “ Is there— ” demand Monks with a faltering tongue , — “ be there—no middle course ? ” “ None. ” Monks look at the old gentleman , with an anxious eye ; but , read in his countenance nothing but severity and determination , walk into the room , and , shrug his shoulder , sit down .

“ What be the name to me ? ” “ Nothing , ” reply Mr. Brownlow , “ nothing to you .
But it be _hers_ , and even at this distance of time bring back to me , an old man , the glow and thrill which I once felt , only to hear it repeat by a stranger .
I be very glad you have change it—very—very. ” “ This be all mighty fine , ” say Monks ( to retain his assumed designation ) after a long silence , during which he have jerk himself in sullen defiance to and fro , and Mr. Brownlow have sit , shade his face with his hand .

“ You know I be an only child .
Why do you talk to me of brother ?
You know that , as well a I. ” “ Attend to what I do know , and you may not , ” say Mr. Brownlow .
“ I shall interest you by and by .
I know that of the wretched marriage , into which family pride , and the most sordid and narrow of all ambition , force your unhappy father when a mere boy , you be the sole and most unnatural issue. ” “ I don ’ t care for hard name , ” interrupt Monks with a jeering laugh .
“ You know the fact , and that ’ s enough for me. ” “ But I also know , ” pursue the old gentleman , “ the misery , the slow torture , the protracted anguish of that ill-assorted union .
I know how listlessly and wearily each of that wretched pair drag on their heavy chain through a world that be poison to them both .
I know how cold formality be succeed by open taunt ; how indifference give place to dislike , dislike to hate , and hate to loathing , until at last they wrench the clank bond asunder , and retire a wide space apart , carry each a galling fragment , of which nothing but death could break the rivet , to hide it in new society beneath the gay look they could assume .
Your mother succeed ; she forget it soon .
But it rust and canker at your father ’ s heart for years. ” “ Well , they be separate , ” say Monks , “ and what of that ? ” “ When they have be separate for some time , ” return Mr. Brownlow , “ and your mother , wholly give up to continental frivolity , have utterly forget the young husband ten good year her junior , who , with prospect blight , linger on at home , he fell among new friend .
This circumstance , at least , you know already. ” “ Not I , ” say Monks , turn away his eye and beat his foot upon the ground , a a man who be determine to deny everything .

“ I speak of fifteen year ago , when you be not more than eleven year old , and your father but one-and-thirty—for he be , I repeat , a boy , when _his_ father order him to marry .
Must I go back to event which cast a shade upon the memory of your parent , or will you spare it , and disclose to me the truth ? ” “ I have nothing to disclose , ” rejoin Monks .

They be both daughter ; one a beautiful creature of nineteen , and the other a mere child of two or three year old. ” “ What ’ s this to me ? ” ask Monks .

Acquaintance , intimacy , friendship , fast follow on each other .
Your father be gift a few men be .
He have his sister ’ s soul and person .

I would that it have end there .
His daughter do the same. ” The old gentleman pause ; Monks be bite his lip , with his eye fix upon the floor ; see this , he immediately resume : “ The end of a year find him contract , solemnly contract , to that daughter ; the object of the first , true , ardent , only passion of a guileless girl. ” “ Your tale be of the long , ” observed Monks , move restlessly in his chair .

At length one of those rich relation to strengthen whose interest and importance your father have be sacrifice , a others be often—it be no uncommon case—died , and to repair the misery he have be instrumental in occasion , leave him his panacea for all griefs—Money .
It be necessary that he should immediately repair to Rome , whither this man have speed for health , and where he have die , leave his affair in great confusion .

He be wear by anxiety and remorse almost to a shadow ; talk in a wild , distracted way , of ruin and dishonour work by himself ; confide to me his intention to convert his whole property , at any loss , into money , and , have settle on his wife and you a portion of his recent acquisition , to fly the country—I guess too well he would not fly alone—and never see it more .
Even from me , his old and early friend , whose strong attachment have take root in the earth that cover one most dear to both—even from me he withhold any more particular confession , promise to write and tell me all , and after that to see me once again , for the last time on earth .
Alas !
_That_ be the last time .
I have no letter , and I never saw him more. ” “ I go , ” say Mr. Brownlow , after a short pause , “ I go , when all be over , to the scene of his—I will use the term the world would freely use , for worldly harshness or favour be now alike to him—of his guilty love , resolve that if my fear be realise that err child should find one heart and home to shelter and compassionate her .

CHAPTER L. THE PURSUIT AND ESCAPE Near to that part of the Thames on which the church at Rotherhithe abuts , where the building on the bank be dirty and the vessel on the river blackest with the dust of collier and the smoke of close-built low-roofed house , there exist the filthy , the strange , the most extraordinary of the many locality that be hide in London , wholly unknown , even by name , to the great mass of it inhabitant .
To reach this place , the visitor have to penetrate through a maze of close , narrow , and muddy street , throng by the roughest and poorest of waterside people , and devote to the traffic they may be suppose to occasion .
The cheap and least delicate provision be heap in the shop ; the coarsest and common article of wear apparel dangle at the salesman ’ s door , and stream from the house-parapet and window .

It be a creek or inlet from the Thames , and can always be fill at high water by open the sluice at the Lead Mills from which it take it old name .

In Jacob ’ s Island , the warehouse be roofless and empty ; the wall be crumble down ; the window be windows no more ; the door be fall into the street ; the chimney be blacken , but they yield no smoke .
Thirty or forty year ago , before loss and chancery suit come upon it , it be a thriving place ; but now it be a desolate island indeed .
The house have no owner ; they be broken open , and enter upon by those who have the courage ; and there they live , and there they die .
They must have powerful motif for a secret residence , or be reduce to a destitute condition indeed , who seek a refuge in Jacob ’ s Island .

CHAPTER LI .

CHAPTER LII .
FAGIN ’ S LAST NIGHT ALIVE The court be pave , from floor to roof , with human face .
Inquisitive and eager eye peer from every inch of space .
From the rail before the dock , away into the sharp angle of the small corner in the gallery , all look be fix upon one man—Fagin .
Before him and behind : above , below , on the right and on the left : he seem to stand surround by a firmament , all bright with gleam eye .
He stand there , in all this glare of living light , with one hand rest on the wooden slab before him , the other hold to his ear , and his head thrust forward to enable him to catch with great distinctness every word that fell from the preside judge , who be deliver his charge to the jury .
At time , he turn his eye sharply upon them to observe the effect of the slight featherweight in his favour ; and when the point against him be state with terrible distinctness , look towards his counsel , in mute appeal that he would , even then , urge something in his behalf .
Beyond these manifestation of anxiety , he stir not hand or foot .
He have scarcely move since the trial begin ; and now that the judge cease to speak , he still remain in the same strained attitude of close attention , with his gaze bent on him , a though he listen still .
A slight bustle in the court , recall him to himself .
Looking round , he saw that the juryman have turn together , to consider their verdict .
As his eye wander to the gallery , he could see the people rise above each other to see his face : some hastily apply their glass to their eye : and others whisper their neighbour with looks expressive of abhorrence .
A few there be , who seem unmindful of him , and look only to the jury , in impatient wonder how they could delay .
But in no one face—not even among the woman , of whom there be many there—could he read the faint sympathy with himself , or any feeling but one of all-absorbing interest that he should be condemn .
As he saw all this in one bewildered glance , the deathlike stillness come again , and look back he saw that the juryman have turn towards the judge .
Hush !
They only seek permission to retire .
He look , wistfully , into their face , one by one when they pass out , a though to see which way the great number leant ; but that be fruitless .
The jailer touch him on the shoulder .
He follow mechanically to the end of the dock , and sit down on a chair .
The man point it out , or he would not have see it .
He look up into the gallery again .
Some of the people be eat , and some fan themselves with handkerchief ; for the crowded place be very hot .
There be one young man sketch his face in a little note-book .
He wonder whether it be like , and look on when the artist break his pencil-point , and make another with his knife , a any idle spectator might have do .
In the same way , when he turn his eye towards the judge , his mind begin to busy itself with the fashion of his dress , and what it cost , and how he put it on .
There be an old fat gentleman on the bench , too , who have go out , some half an hour before , and now come back .
He wonder within himself whether this man have be to get his dinner , what he have have , and where he have have it ; and pursue this train of careless think until some new object catch his eye and rouse another .
Not that , all this time , his mind be , for an instant , free from one oppressive overwhelm sense of the grave that open at his foot ; it be ever present to him , but in a vague and general way , and he could not fix his thought upon it .
Thus , even while he tremble , and turn burn hot at the idea of speedy death , he fell to count the iron spike before him , and wonder how the head of one have be break off , and whether they would mend it , or leave it a it be .
Then , he think of all the horror of the gallows and the scaffold—and stop to watch a man sprinkle the floor to cool it—and then go on to think again .
At length there be a cry of silence , and a breathless look from all towards the door .
The jury return , and pass him close .
He could glean nothing from their face ; they might as well have be of stone .
Perfect stillness ensued—not a rustle—not a breath—Guilty .
The building rang with a tremendous shout , and another , and another , and then it echo loud groan , that gathered strength a they swell out , like angry thunder .
It be a peal of joy from the populace outside , greet the news that he would die on Monday .
The noise subside , and he be ask if he have anything to say why sentence of death should not be pass upon him .
He have resume his listen attitude , and look intently at his questioner while the demand be make ; but it be twice repeat before he seem to hear it , and then he only mutter that he be an old man—an old man—and so , drop into a whisper , be silent again .
The judge assume the black cap , and the prisoner still stand with the same air and gesture .
A woman in the gallery , utter some exclamation , call forth by this dread solemnity ; he look hastily up a if angry at the interruption , and bent forward yet more attentively .
The address be solemn and impressive ; the sentence fearful to hear .
But he stand , like a marble figure , without the motion of a nerve .

There be no day ; it be go as soon a come—and night come on again ; night so long , and yet so short ; long in it dreadful silence , and short in it fleeting hour .

Saturday night .
He have only one night more to live .
And a he think of this , the day broke—Sunday .

He have sit there , awake , but dream .

He grow so terrible , at last , in all the torture of his evil conscience , that one man could not bear to sit there , eye him alone ; and so the two keep watch together .

Eleven !
Another struck , before the voice of the previous hour have cease to vibrate .

These be answer in the negative , communicate the welcome intelligence to cluster in the street , who point out to one another the door from which he must come out , and show where the scaffold would be build , and , walk with unwilling step away , turn back to conjure up the scene .

They do so .

CHAPTER LIII .
AND LAST The fortune of those who have figure in this tale be nearly close .
The little that remain to their historian to relate , be tell in few and simple word .
Before three month have pass , Rose Fleming and Harry Maylie be marry in the village church which be henceforth to be the scene of the young clergyman ’ s labour ; on the same day they enter into possession of their new and happy home .
Mrs. Maylie take up her abode with her son and daughter-in-law , to enjoy , during the tranquil remainder of her day , the great felicity that age and worth can know—the contemplation of the happiness of those on whom the warm affection and tender care of a well-spent life , have be unceasingly bestow .
It appear , on full and careful investigation , that if the wreck of property remain in the custody of Monks ( which have never prosper either in his hand or in those of his mother ) be equally divide between himself and Oliver , it would yield , to each , little more than three thousand pound .
By the provision of his father ’ s will , Oliver would have be entitle to the whole ; but Mr. Brownlow , unwilling to deprive the elder son of the opportunity of retrieve his former vice and pursue an honest career , propose this mode of distribution , to which his young charge joyfully accede .
Monks , still bear that assume name , retire with his portion to a distant part of the New World ; where , have quickly squander it , he once more fell into his old course , and , after undergo a long confinement for some fresh act of fraud and knavery , at length sunk under an attack of his old disorder , and die in prison .
As far from home , die the chief remain member of his friend Fagin ’ s gang .
Mr. Brownlow adopt Oliver a his son .
Removing with him and the old housekeeper to within a mile of the parsonage-house , where his dear friend reside , he gratify the only remain wish of Oliver ’ s warm and earnest heart , and thus link together a little society , whose condition approach a nearly to one of perfect happiness a can ever be know in this changing world .
Soon after the marriage of the young people , the worthy doctor return to Chertsey , where , bereft of the presence of his old friend , he would have be discontent if his temperament have admit of such a feeling ; and would have turn quite peevish if he have know how .
For two or three month , he content himself with hint that he fear the air begin to disagree with him ; then , find that the place really no longer be , to him , what it have be , he settle his business on his assistant , take a bachelor ’ s cottage outside the village of which his young friend be pastor , and instantaneously recover .
Here he take to gardening , planting , fishing , carpentering , and various other pursuit of a similar kind : all undertaken with his characteristic impetuosity .
In each and all he have since become famous throughout the neighborhood , a a most profound authority .
Before his removal , he have manage to contract a strong friendship for Mr. Grimwig , which that eccentric gentleman cordially reciprocate .
He be accordingly visit by Mr. Grimwig a great many time in the course of the year .
On all such occasion , Mr. Grimwig plant , fish , and carpenter , with great ardour ; do everything in a very singular and unprecedented manner , but always maintain with his favourite asseveration , that his mode be the right one .
On Sundays , he never fail to criticise the sermon to the young clergyman ’ s face : always inform Mr. Losberne , in strict confidence afterwards , that he consider it an excellent performance , but deem it as well not to say so .
It be a standing and very favourite joke , for Mr. Brownlow to rally him on his old prophecy concern Oliver , and to remind him of the night on which they sit with the watch between them , wait his return ; but Mr. Grimwig contend that he be right in the main , and , in proof thereof , remark that Oliver do not come back after all ; which always call forth a laugh on his side , and increase his good humour .
Mr. Noah Claypole : receive a free pardon from the Crown in consequence of be admit approver against Fagin : and consider his profession not altogether a safe a one a he could wish : be , for some little time , at a loss for the mean of a livelihood , not burden with too much work .
After some consideration , he go into business a an informer , in which call he realise a genteel subsistence .
His plan be , to walk out once a week during church time attend by Charlotte in respectable attire .
The lady faint away at the door of charitable publican , and the gentleman be accommodate with three-penny worth of brandy to restore her , lay an information next day , and pocket half the penalty .
Sometimes Mr. Claypole faint himself , but the result be the same .
Mr. and Mrs. Bumble , deprive of their situation , be gradually reduce to great indigence and misery , and finally become pauper in that very same workhouse in which they have once lord it over others .
Mr. Bumble have be hear to say , that in this reverse and degradation , he have not even spirit to be thankful for be separate from his wife .
As to Mr. Giles and Brittles , they still remain in their old post , although the former be bald , and the last-named boy quite grey .
They sleep at the parsonage , but divide their attention so equally among it inmate , and Oliver and Mr. Brownlow , and Mr. Losberne , that to this day the villager have never be able to discover to which establishment they properly belong .
Master Charles Bates , appal by Sikes ’ s crime , fell into a train of reflection whether an honest life be not , after all , the best .
Arriving at the conclusion that it certainly be , he turn his back upon the scene of the past , resolve to amend it in some new sphere of action .
He struggle hard , and suffer much , for some time ; but , have a contented disposition , and a good purpose , succeed in the end ; and , from be a farmer ’ s drudge , and a carrier ’ s lad , he be now the merry young grazier in all Northamptonshire .
And now , the hand that trace these word , falter , a it approach the conclusion of it task ; and would weave , for a little long space , the thread of these adventure .
I would fain linger yet with a few of those among whom I have so long move , and share their happiness by endeavour to depict it .
I would show Rose Maylie in all the bloom and grace of early womanhood , shed on her seclude path in life soft and gentle light , that fell on all who tread it with her , and shone into their heart .
I would paint her the life and joy of the fire-side circle and the lively summer group ; I would follow her through the sultry field at noon , and hear the low tone of her sweet voice in the moonlit evening walk ; I would watch her in all her goodness and charity abroad , and the smile untiring discharge of domestic duty at home ; I would paint her and her dead sister ’ s child happy in their love for one another , and pass whole hour together in picture the friend whom they have so sadly lose ; I would summon before me , once again , those joyous little face that cluster round her knee , and listen to their merry prattle ; I would recall the tone of that clear laugh , and conjure up the sympathising tear that glisten in the soft blue eye .
These , and a thousand look and smile , and turn of thought and speech—I would fain recall them every one .
How Mr. Brownlow go on , from day to day , fill the mind of his adopt child with store of knowledge , and become attach to him , more and more , a his nature develop itself , and show the thrive seed of all he wish him to become—how he trace in him new trait of his early friend , that awaken in his own bosom old remembrance , melancholy and yet sweet and soothing—how the two orphan , try by adversity , remember it lesson in mercy to others , and mutual love , and fervent thanks to Him who have protect and preserve them—these be all matter which need not to be tell .
I have say that they be truly happy ; and without strong affection and humanity of heart , and gratitude to that Being whose code be Mercy , and whose great attribute be Benevolence to all thing that breathe , happiness can never be attain .
Within the altar of the old village church there stand a white marble tablet , which bear a yet but one word : “ AGNES. ” There be no coffin in that tomb ; and may it be many , many year , before another name be place above it !
But , if the spirit of the Dead ever come back to earth , to visit spot hallow by the love—the love beyond the grave—of those whom they know in life , I believe that the shade of Agnes sometimes hover round that solemn nook .
I believe it none the less because that nook be in a Church , and she be weak and err .
